ANNEX
Questions to the Council
Question no 1 by Alexandros Alavanos (H-0133/01)
 Subject: Increase in the European Union budget
 

The second report on economic and social cohesion in the EU points out the wide disparities in development between the Member States, the regions and the countries which will be joining the EU in the next few years. In order to reduce these disparities, or to prevent the gap from widening at least, a much greater amount of funding than that provided by the EU budget will be required. At the same time, the cost of dealing with the BSE crisis has raised spending on agriculture to its maximum permissible level, prohibiting any other action and necessitating cutbacks in other areas of agricultural expenditure.

Since it is becoming increasingly clear that the EU's budget is unable to meet the needs of enlargement or cover current EU policies, will the Council consider increasing the EU's own resources? Have the Member States raised the question of increasing own resources or revising the financial perspective? How will the Council satisfy these urgent financial needs?

 
  
 

The Council calls upon the honourable Member to study the answer already given at Question Time at the last part-session in February. This answer was sent to him in writing and must be regarded as the answer that the Council is still giving to this question. A copy of it will again be sent to him.

 

Question no 11 by Mihail Papayannakis (H-0161/01)
 Subject: Statements by the Presidency on behalf of the EU
 

One of the countries which has been countersigning statements by the Presidency on behalf of the EU within the framework of the common foreign policy since the beginning of December 2000 is Turkey. So far, this country has countersigned all the statements in which the associated countries have been involved. However, in the statement of 2 February 2001, (5697/01 - Press release 36), on the case of Mr Felix Kulof, Turkey's signature is missing. On the basis of what mechanism do the associated countries sign statements by the Presidency? Do they have the right to choose which statements they sign, and how is this choice accepted? How credible is the entire system of participation if those concerned are able to choose which statements they sign and what they condemn (for instance, human rights issues), depending on the practices within their own countries?

 
  
 

In line with the conclusions of the European Council of Helsinki in December, Turkey - like the Central and Eastern European countries associated with the European Union, the associated countries Cyprus and Malta, and the European Free Trade Associates (EFTA) countries, Iceland, Liechtenstein and Norway, members of the European Economic Area - is invited to align itself with CFSP declarations made by the Presidency on behalf of the European Union.

One cannot expect countries - even candidates for EU accession - to associate themselves automatically with EU declarations on foreign policy issues on which they have not been consulted and in the drafting of which they have played no part. On the other hand, as part of the process by which they approach accession the EU welcomes it when they feel able to associate themselves with EU positions in CFSP and indeed encourages them to do so. But it must be for these countries to decide freely whether they wish to do so.

In 2000, the associates were invited to associate themselves with 131 CFSP declarations. In 95 cases, all countries concerned aligned themselves; in 36 cases, one or more countries wished not to do so, in 18 cases of which Turkey was one of them. In 2001, the Associated countries have been invited to align themselves with 16 declarations. Turkey has aligned itself with 15 of these declarations.

 

Question no 12 by Ursula Schleicher (H-0162/01)
 Subject: Suspension of gas supplies from Russia
 

The Russian government has ordered a stop to gas supplies from Georgia. The European Union is currently heavily dependent on Russian gas. Does the Council agree that Russia has shown that it is not a reliable supplier of energy to the EU?

 
  
 

The Council is not aware of the commercial modalities of the gas supply agreements between Georgia and Russia.

However, the Council is concerned about the interruption of gas supplies from Russia to Georgia and its effect on the economic, social and humanitarian situation in Georgia. Interruptions of this kind, however short, do not only affect the people directly involved but, of course, in a wider perspective, also have an effect on Russia’s reputation as a gas supplier.

The interest of the Council in increasing the security of energy supplies is demonstrated by several items on the agenda of a forthcoming Council meeting.

Firstly, the Council is currently undertaking an in-depth examination of the Commission's Green Paper: "Towards a European strategy for the security of supply", which underlines our growing dependence on external energy sources and notably on Russia for gas. Within the context of enlargement, this dependence will increase further due to the historic links between Eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union. One of the responses to dependence will be increased diversification of energy sources.

Moreover, on the occasion of the 6th EU-Russia summit since the entry into force (1.12.1997) of the EU-Russian Partnership and Cooperation Agreement (PCA), held in Paris on 30 October 2000, it was agreed to institute an energy dialogue on a regular basis to enable progress to be made in the definition and arrangements for an EU-Russian Energy Partnership. Since then, exploratory contacts at high level have been taking place, taking into account the interest of the EU in safeguarding the continuation of reliable energy supplies from Russia. On the same occasion, Russia also confirmed its commitment to proceed to the ratification of the Energy Charter Treaty in the near future. The Community is currently participating in negotiations on a Transit Protocol to the Energy Charter Treaty, which should also contribute to increasing the security of supply, especially as regards the legal framework.

Finally, the Action Plan on the Northern Dimension, adopted at the European Council of Feira, 19­20 June 2000, comprises, within a series of priority actions, measures to ensure (legal) security of investment and of supply from i.a. Northwest Russia. Implementation of this Action Plan will be reviewed at the June European Council in Göteborg.

 

Question no 13 by Camilo Nogueira Román (H-0164/01)
 Subject: The civilian population in Chechnya
 

What action is the Council taking in support of the civilian population in Chechnya, given that credible sources state that it is now the victim of a tragic wave of unexplained disappearances, illegal arrests and assassinations attributable to the Russian military, while the legal authorities of the Russian Federation are failing in their duty to protect the most basic human rights?

 
  
 

The Council remains concerned at the difficult situation of the civilian population in Chechnya. In all political dialogue meetings with Russia, as well as in the OSCE and Council of Europe, the EU expresses its serious concerns at reports on violations of human rights and humanitarian law, in particular forced disappearances, summary executions, torture and arbitrary detentions. The EU is keeping the pressure on the Russian authorities to bring human rights violations before the court. The Council regularly expresses its disappointment that only very few complaints received by Mr Kalamanov have been followed by criminal proceedings so far.

Furthermore, the Council keeps stressing to Russia that the return of the OSCE Assistance Group to Chechnya is important and that it should be able to fulfil its full mandate. Foreign Minister Ivanov recently expressed Russia's agreement to the return of the Assistance Group. Only a few technical questions remain to be resolved. The Council will watch closely to see if Ivanov's statement is followed by concrete steps.

In view of the Commission for human rights in Geneva (19-27 March), the EU is to bring the human rights situation in Chechnya before the Commission, considering that the recommendations from last year's Commission have not been fully implemented and that the human rights situation remains unsatisfactory.

As long as the situation in Chechnya does not substantially improve, the Council will insist on discussing this point at all political dialogue meetings and it will explain that the lack of progress on Chechnya remains an obstacle to developing the full scope of the envisaged EU-Russia partnership. Upcoming opportunities to express the Council's concern are the Cooperation Council with Russia on 10 April and the EU-Russia Summit on 17 May.

 

Question no 14 by Gerard Collins (H-0168/01)
 Subject: Campaign against poverty in the world
 

Central to its programme for effective development cooperation, the Swedish Presidency is committed to the campaign against poverty. Will the Council make a statement on its efforts in this area, including its position on the elimination of Third World debt?

 
  
 

The Council as a whole is deeply committed to the fight against poverty. We believe that we possess the knowledge and resources to make substantial progress towards the international development goal of halving the proportion of people living in extreme poverty by the year 2015. You will remember that this goal was confirmed by 149 Heads of State and Government at the UN Millennium Summit last autumn.

In its joint declaration with the Commission on 10 November 2000, the Council stressed that the main objective of Community development policy must be to reduce and, eventually, to eradicate poverty. Poverty is a multidimensional phenomenon. Experience shows that poverty is not just a matter of lack of material resources – it is also a lack of access to power. To be poor means not being able to make one's voice heard. It is to feel insecure. To be poor is to have very little chance of influencing one's own life. This implies that poverty includes much more than the conventional dimensions of income or consumption. Poverty also includes risk, vulnerability, voicelessness and powerlessness. The fight against poverty must take all these dimensions into consideration. The perception of the poor themselves must be the starting point.

Bringing the different dimensions into our understanding of poverty inevitably leads us to see its context specificity.

Accordingly, in their declaration, the Council and the Commission went on to discuss ways of combating poverty, highlighting the importance of measures to improve:

Access to adequate food supplies, health and education, natural resources and drinking water, land, employment and credit facilities, information and communication technologies, political involvement, services and infrastructure. The Council also emphasised the need for consolidating the democratic process, respect for human rights, peace and the prevention of conflict, the development of social policies and the integration of social and environmental concerns in macro-economic reform programmes.

Another important dimension of development is the gravity of the communicable diseases, especially HIV/AIDS. In view of the global dimension of the problem and its impact on poverty, targeting this problem must be considered an absolute necessity.

Furthermore, the declaration stressed the integration of developing countries, in particular the least developed among them, into the world economy. In this respect, the Council recognised that economic growth was essential, although not sufficient in itself to ensure poverty reduction.

The Council also supported the Commission’s view that Community activities should be concentrated in a limited number of areas selected on the basis of their contribution towards reducing poverty and for which Community action provides added value. These are trade, regional integration and cooperation, support to macroeconomic framework, transport, food security and institutional capacity building.

As a follow-up to the new development policy, the Commission has, on several occasions, stated that it will, early 2001, deliver a communication on how to strengthen its capacity to address the objective of poverty reduction in the EC development cooperation. The Swedish Presidency is of firm belief that the Commission will live up to its commitment in this area. Poverty cannot be handled as a sector or separate part of development cooperation. To implement the development policy, the interdisciplinary and poverty focused approach to development partnerships must be strengthened. There is also a need for improved coherence among different policy areas focusing on poverty alleviation. Increasing competence and capacity of the Commission to fully embrace the poverty reduction objective is a means to achieve this.

As regards the elimination of third world debt, which is obviously relevant to the fight against poverty, the Council would refer to its answer to oral question H-0108/01 from Mr Brian Crowley.

 

Question no 15 by Niall Andrews (H-0170/01)
 Subject: Afghanistan
 

There is growing alarm at the situation in Afghanistan. People are dying in the camps, and the majority are children. The UN has warned of an imminent devastating crisis after two years of drought, war and lack of investment. Aid workers consider that UN sanctions against Afghanistan have disrupted the distribution of relief supplies. Will the Council therefore lead a move to end those sanctions?

 
  
 

Sanctions against the Taliban - and not against Afghanistan - have been adopted by the UN Security Council following the Taliban's refusal to extradite Usama bin Laden, who is abusing his host country as a basis to launch acts of international terrorism and as a convenient cover to set up a network of training camps for radical terrorist groups. The relevant UNSC Resolution 1267 (1999) further accuses the Taliban of, inter alia, massive violations of human rights and of international humanitarian law. The sanctions adopted concern a flight ban for aircraft under Taliban control and a freezing of Taliban assets.

As a result of the Taliban's persistent refusal to comply with the pertinent UNSC Resolutions, the UNSC adopted, in December 2000, its Resolution 1333 (2000), which oblige all UN Member States to adopt a new set of sanctions against the Taliban regime. These sanctions demand in particular: the prohibition of direct or indirect supply, sale and transfer of arms and related military material; the prohibition of the direct or indirect supply, sale and transfer of technical advise, assistance or training related to military activities of Taliban forces; the withdrawal of military experts and advisors from the Taliban regime; the closure of Taliban and of Ariana airlines offices; the freezing of assets of Usama bin Laden an individuals or entities associated with him; the prohibition of the sale, supply or transfer of the chemical acetic anhydride (necessary for the production of Heroin).

The Council, while fully sharing the honourable MEP's preoccupations concerning the disastrous humanitarian situation in Afghanistan, does not believe that these sanctions have or will disrupt the distribution of relief supplies. On the contrary, these targeted sanctions are aimed against a regime which, due to its ruthless pursuit of the civil war and of its massive violations of human rights and the international humanitarian law, is primarily responsible for the disastrous situation in Afghanistan. The severe drought has contributed to a further deterioration of the humanitarian situation. The Council expects that the sanctions will contribute to reduce outside interference and to promote a negotiated settlement of the Afghan conflict. The Council further expects that they will lead to a reduction in the production of Afghan drugs, thus reducing the revenue necessary for the Taliban to pursue and to finance the war.

In the meantime, the EU will continue to provide humanitarian aid to internally displaced Afghans and to Afghan refugees in neighbouring countries, whenever the difficult conditions on the ground allow.

 

Question no 16 by Brian Crowley (H-0172/01)
 Subject: Clostridium novyi bacterium and heroin deaths
 

Deaths caused in Ireland, Scotland and England by contaminated heroin are being linked to the toxin producing strain of Clostridium novyi type A which is a very virulent organism. This 'bad batch' of heroin may still be available. Health Boards in Ireland and Scotland collaborated in the investigations, as did Centres for Disease Control and Prevention in the US. Will the Council state what lessons it has learned from this case, and does it agree that the tragic deaths arising from such heroin use should strengthen further the EU’s resolve to fight drugs?

 
  
 

The Council informs the honourable Member that the matter referred to in the question has not been discussed in the framework of the Council.

A programme of Community action on the prevention of drug dependence within the framework action in the field of public health (1996-2000)1 was recently extended until 31 December 2001. The objective of this programme is to help in combating drug dependence, in particular by encouraging cooperation between Member States, supporting their action and promoting coordination between their policies and programmes, while ensuring consistency and complementarity with other relevant community programmes. It also provides for cooperation with non-member countries and relevant international organisations. The actions of the programme centre on the collection of data, research and evaluation as well as on information, education and training.

The programme will be replaced by the programme of Community action in the field of public health, presently being examined by Council.

Finally as the honourable Member knows the European Union attaches great attention to the fight against drugs. Thus, the EU Action Plan on Drugs (2000 - 2004) was drawn up and endorsed by the European Council at its recent June meeting in Feira. Collection, analysis and dissemination of objective, reliable and comparable data on the drugs phenomenon with support of EMCDDA and Europol, as well as drug prevention, demand reduction and the reduction of the adverse consequences of drug use are among the priority activities listed in the Action Plan.

 

Question no 17 by James (Jim) Fitzsimons (H-0174/01)
 Subject: Clean energy
 

Will the Council set out its views on the establishment of new initiatives aimed at facilitating a switch from the use of fossil fuels to greater use of clean energy sources, and does it consider that the recent severe winter storms across Europe confirm the need for such initiatives?

 
  
 

The honourable Member will certainly remember that in his recent speech delivered to the Committee on Industry, External Trade, Research and Energy of the European Parliament (23.1.2001), the President of the Council has indicated the high priority it attaches to initiatives contributing to a greater use of non-fossil energy sources. As the Council had already the opportunity to do in its answer given to the question raised by Mary Honeyball during Question Time last February, it recalls the ongoing work in the following areas:

Following last December's Council (Industry/Energy) agreement on a political orientation on the proposed Directive for the promotion of electricity from renewable energy sources in the internal market, the Council will, within the coming weeks, formally adopt its common position on this text, which should confirm the commitment of Member States to a indicative Community target of electricity from renewable energy sources of 22.1% by the year 2010. This Directive will represent a significant progress in favour of electricity from renewable energy sources, all the more needed given the difficulties faced by the Climate change negotiations.

The Council (Industry/Energy) has welcomed with much interest the presentation of the Commission's Green Paper: "Towards a European strategy for the security of energy supply" at its December meeting. This paper, launched to raise an overall debate, proposes a strategy for dealing with the increasing dependence of the Union with regard to energy supply over a 20-year period. Within this strategy, as proposed by the Commission the enhancement of diversification of energy supplies and sources is an important part, and includes the increased efforts in the research of (alternative) new and clean energy sources (ies).

A comprehensive European sustainable development strategy will be prepared with a view to its examination by the European Council (Göteborg) in June 2001. To that effect, the Council, on the basis of a report from the Commission, will be examining the ongoing work on the strengthening of the strategy for the integration of environmental aspects and sustainable development into the energy sector (policy), as adopted at the European Council in Helsinki (1999). This examination will also include consideration of the Commission's Action Plan to improve energy efficiency as well as the proposal for a 6th Community Action Plan on sustainable development. It is our intention to draw some conclusions at the May Energy/Industry Council, in order to bring this work forward. Moreover, we expect that a number of actions will be launched under the Action Plan on energy efficiency on the basis of the priorities identified by the Council at its meeting on 5 December last.

Furthermore the Council recalls that among existing instruments for the promotion of cleaner energy, (the ALTENER and CARNOT subprogrammes of the Energy), the framework programme on Energy, i.e. ALTENER, SAVE, ETAP and CARNOT, is of specific (are particularly) importance (t).

Last but not least, as regards the relation between clean energy and physical disruptions, the Council considers that the progress towards the achievement of a fully operational internal energy market, as stated in the Lisbon European Council conclusions, will contribute to diminishing the influence of external factors on security of supply.

 

Question no 18 by Pat the Cope Gallagher (H-0176/01)
 Subject: Childcare institutions in Romania
 

Will the Swedish Council Presidency outline what priority it attaches to securing a comprehensive reform of Romania’s childcare institutions and what emphasis it is placing on this issue in the context of the overall enlargement process?

 
  
 

The Council has already had the opportunity, in October last year, to assure the honourable Member that it attaches the highest priority to an improvement in the situation in Romania's childcare institutions. This commitment will continue under the Swedish and future Presidencies.

The Commission's 2000 regular report on Romania's progress towards accession concluded that, "after some delay, adequate budgetary provisions have been made for institutionalised children and a strategy for a comprehensive reform of child care policies has been launched". However, the report identified a number of shortcomings in this area and noted that "the key issue for the Romanian authorities will be to ensure that this strategy is successfully implemented; so as to allow an improvement in the actual living conditions".

The European Union will continue to take every opportunity to emphasise to the Romanian authorities the importance of tackling this issue. The subject will, in particular, be raised at the EU-Romania Association Council on 19 March 2001.

 

Question no 19 by Liam Hyland (H-0178/01)
 Subject: Identifying and supporting new growth areas in farming
 

Given the ongoing crisis affecting the farming sector and the knock-on effect on related service sectors in the Community, will the Council outline its strategy for guaranteeing a real future for farming, for young farmers and family farms, and will it give an undertaking that, as part of that strategy, new areas with potential for agricultural growth will be identified and supported?

 
  
 

Having regard to the evolution of the social and economic situation of the farming sector in the European Union, the Council endeavors to improve the common agricultural policy while taking into account the increasingly competitive international environment, the rapid evolution of science and technology in a society more and more concerned about environmental protection, food quality and sustainable development.

The ongoing BSE- and Foot-and-Mouth disease crisis affecting the farming sector and the knock-on effect which this has on related services sectors in the Community will inevitably lead to new approaches for the fulfilment of the objectives of the CAP being considered in particular in identifying and promoting new growth areas in farming, including the encouragement of consumer and environmentally friendly production as well as the development of a more diversified rural economy.

As already stated in the written reply given to the honourable Member question H-112/01 in February 2001 concerning measures to halt the reduction in agricultural employment, the Council will devote all its attention to any Commission proposals furthering sustainable development of European agriculture. In this way, it may better guarantee a real future for farming, for young farmers and family farms while defining a better framework for rural development. Furthermore, the Council hopes that the national authorities - which can already use for this purpose the facilities of the Regulation 1257/99 - will implement those measures while elaborating programmes and projects ensuring the cofinancing of such opportunities.

 

Question no 20 by Bart Staes (H-0181/01)
 Subject: Operating costs in the fisheries sector
 

Increased operating costs, especially fuel costs, are resulting in problems for the European fisheries sector. Accordingly, some Member States of the EU are intervening in order to reduce fuel costs for shipowners. Such divergent national and/or regional support measures result in a distortion of competition. A coordinated European approach is therefore required.

Will the Council ensure that a coordinated European approach is adopted to the increased operating costs in the fisheries sector, given that the divergent support measures taken by national and/or regional authorities result in a distortion of competition? If not, why will the Council not adopt a coordinated European approach?

 
  
 

On the Presidency's initiative, the Council, at its meeting in November last year, held an exchange of views on the subject of the effects of the increase in the price of fuel on fisheries undertakings.

The Council has, however, received no proposal from the Community for any particular action in this respect. It would recall that any national measures must comply with the Community's competition rules and rules on state aid. Such measures should be notified to the Commission, be degressive and limited in time.

Regarding the Council's general attitude towards subsidies within the energy sector, the decision in ECOFIN on 12 February 2001 should be noted. The directive on mineral oils, which includes harmonised rules for the taxation of mineral oils, allows for Council decisions regarding certain exemptions from those rules, whereby a unanimous decision in the Council is required. Some of the older exemptions expired by the end of 2000, while others were automatically extended. ECOFIN took the decision that, from now on, all exemptions should be time-limited and should not be subject to automatic extension. Most exemptions have now been extended for six years while some, which concern tax refunds for diesel in the transport sector, will only be extended for two years.

 

Question no 21 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H-0183/01)
 Subject: Prospects for women in Iran
 

What progress has been made in Iran over the past four years as regards improving women's living conditions, rights and freedoms? What projects will be carried out to enhance women's social status? Will the 'trade liberalisation' for which preparations are now being made be accompanied by a 'personal liberalisation' that will include women?

 
  
 

The Council remains concerned about the discrimination in law and in practice against women in Iran, for example:

– Human rights, including the situation of women and girls, are an important item in the political dialogue with Iran.

– The European Union is considering, as in previous years, tabling a resolution on the human rights situation in Iran at the UN Commission for Human Rights in Geneva. Such a resolution will include references to the situation of Iranian women and girls.

– The EU supports the call by the UN Special Representative on Iran on all branches of the Government of Iran to take further measures to ensure full and equal enjoyment by women and girls of their human rights.

The Council welcomes the efforts made, in particular by the Iranian Parliament, to improve the status of women and girls in Iran. It regrets that these efforts in many cases have not yet been translated into law. As a matter of fact, in law, women are discriminated against in many different ways, mainly based on Islamic law: inheritance (only half of that of a man), right of divorce, legal testimony, freedom of movement (permission to leave the country), etc. In practice, women are also discriminated against on the labour market (lower wages).

At the same time, the EU recognises that the situation of women is in many respects better in Iran than in many of the neighbouring countries. Women enjoy full legal rights to participate in political life in Iran; there are several women in the Iranian Parliament and some in important positions in the Government, such as one Vice-President. Women practice almost all professions - lawyers, journalists, doctors, business people, teachers; 60 per cent of university students are women.

On 7 February 2001, the Commission adopted a Communication setting out the perspectives for developing closer relations with Iran. This could lead to the conclusion of a Trade and Cooperation Agreement between the EU and Iran wherein an article on human rights would be included.

 

Question no 22 by Esko Olavi Seppänen (H-0184/01)
 Subject: Mandate and intended use of rapid reaction force
 

By decision of the European Council, a rapid reaction force – or European army – is being set up for use by the Union. Has the Council decided what kind of mandate will be required before the force is sent into combat? In other words, will a mandate always have to be secured from the international community, for example as represented by the UN and OSCE, and has it been decided for what kind of purposes or what operations the force is to be used?

 
  
 

The rapid reaction force, as it is called, has nothing to do with a European army. The European Council has emphasised that the capacity now being developed so that the EU can engage in crisis management operations does not in any way mean that a European army is being created. The fact is that, in a case where the Council decided to deploy troops in a crisis management operation, the Member States themselves would have to decide whether or not they intended to contribute national forces to such an operation. The decision to commit forces would have to be made by sovereign national governments.

The European Union has undertaken to respect the principles of the UN Charter, together with the principles of the Final Act of the Helsinki Agreement and the objectives of the Paris Charter. The Union also acknowledges that the UN Security Council has primary responsibility for maintaining international peace and security.

The types of operation to which the rapid reaction force, as it is called, can be committed are clearly defined in the Treaty itself (Article 17.2 of the Treaty on European Union) and include the following:

– humanitarian and rescue tasks,

– peace-keeping tasks,

– tasks of combat forces in crisis management, including peacemaking.

Does the Council plan sanctions against Ireland?

Question no 25 by José Ribeiro e Castro (H-0197/01)
 Subject: Enlargement - Poland - abortion law
 

According to reports in the Polish press, the Swedish Government's Minister for Equal Opportunities, Mrs Margareta Winberg, has strongly criticised Poland on account of the fact that, in that country, abortion is banned by law. Mrs Winberg's criticisms were expressed directly to a Polish Government Under-Secretary of State, Mrs Irena Boruta, who had been invited to Norrköping in order to attend an informal meeting of EU Social Affairs Ministers, together with representatives of other applicant countries.

Mrs Winberg made her comments in her official capacity, against the background of the Swedish presidency and in the context of the informal meeting of EU Ministers which was held at the end of January. Her exact words were allegedly as follows: 'It is unacceptable that a country which is knocking on the European Union's door should behave in such a way'. Mrs Boruta is said to have replied (again according to press reports) that the ban imposed under Polish law had been established by democratic means.

Is the Council aware of the attitude adopted by Mrs Winberg towards Poland and can it confirm the facts as reported? If so, does Mrs Winberg's attitude reflect the Council's official view? On what legal basis? Has the Council any prejudice against enlargement of the EU to include Poland or against democracy in Poland?

 
 

Question no 26 by Rijk van Dam (H-0209/01)
 Subject: Enlargement and abortion law
 

The Polish law of 7 January 1993 protects the life of unborn children and/or foetuses. The number of abortions in that applicant country has declined sharply: from more than 100 000 per year until 1988 to a few hundred in recent years. Representatives of the Presidency and of the EP have criticised the Polish law prohibiting abortion. As penal law is not subject to the ‘acquis’, does such a law have any relevance in negotiations with applicant countries, and would it play a role in the accession process?

Is the Council planning to include the issue of abortion law in the EU information campaign being carried out in Poland to prepare that country for membership or in any activity with that in mind?

Joint Answer

The Council would like to inform the honourable Members that it is not in the habit of expressing its views on the content of statements made by national ministers.

The Council believes that Poland continues to fulfil the political Copenhagen accession criteria, with which the applicant countries must have complied and which relate, namely, to stable institutions guaranteeing democracy, the rule of law, human rights and respect for, and the protection of, minorities.

With regard to the special issue of abortion, the Council would remind the honourable Members that national abortion legislation does not fall within the Community’s competence. The issue of conformity with the Community’s regulations does not therefore arise.

With regard to Ireland, the Council also wishes to remind the honourable Members of Protocol No 17, annexed to the Treaty of European Union and the Treaty establishing the European Community, and of the Declaration of 1 May 1992 of the High Contracting Parties to the Treaty on European Union, which constitutes a legal interpretation of that Protocol.

The Council would remind the honourable Member that, in the Nice conclusions on 7–11 December 2000, the European Council insisted upon the historic significance of the European Union’s enlargement process, together with the political priority to be given to making this process succeed. The Council has welcomed the fact that the accession negotiations with the applicant countries have been intensified. These have led to very important progress, especially in recent months. This is naturally inconsistent with any suspicion of prejudice against any of the applicant countries.

 
 

Question no 27 by Lennart Sacrédeus (H-0190/01)
 Subject: Political disappearances in Belarus
 

During the January part-session, the Swedish Presidency answered a question I had asked concerning the situation in Belarus – a neighbour of the three applicant countries Latvia, Lithuania and Poland – and the measures and steps to be taken to clarify the fate of three missing persons, namely the former Interior Minister Jurij Zaherenko, former Deputy Chairman Victor Gonchar, and the Russian film-maker, Dmitrij Zavadskij. The answer also stressed that the Swedish Presidency would resolutely pursue the previous Council presidencies' endeavours to promote the democratic development of Belarus.

Two more months have now passed and none of the missing persons have yet been found. Will the Swedish Presidency, therefore, say whether its efforts have given any indication or shed any light at all on whether an answer will be found to the disturbing disappearance of these three people?

 
  
 

The Council continues to be concerned about the lack of democracy in Belarus in general and the disappearances of Mr Zakharenko, Mr Gonchar and Mr Zavadzky in particular. It considers a period of truce with the political opposition in Belarus, including investigations of the cases of disappeared persons, as an important measure for democratic development in the country.

A Regional Director's Troika in February carried out a démarche in Minsk to urge the Belarusian authorities to create the necessary conditions for democratic elections. The Troika notably pressed the authorities to provide information about the disappearances. Belarus stressed that investigations were under way, but had not led to any concrete result yet.

The EU will continue to press Belarus in this regard on every occasion, notably through its Heads of Mission in Minsk. It will also pursue its TACIS projects for the promotion of democracy in Belarus, supporting civil society in particular. The EU will continue to support the valuable efforts of the OSCE in Belarus, including its efforts to shed light on the disappearances.

 

Question no 28 by Rosa Miguélez Ramos (H-0192/01)
 Subject: Aid for the livestock sector in countries affected by BSE
 

In recent years, cases of animals affected by bovine spongiform encephalopathy have been discovered in various countries after the United Kingdom had exported animal meal to them at a time when sale of the product had already been banned in the UK.

At the time when it occurred, the European Union granted the United Kingdom substantial aid to tackle the crisis. Now that other states are experiencing cases of mad cow disease, pubic opinion expects similar Community aid to that offered to the country which was at the origin of the disease. Otherwise, we shall again be faced with the paradox, described by President Prodi when presenting the Commission’s work programme for 2001 to the European Parliament, of a Europe that is ever more necessary yet which is seen as ever more remote.

What funding did the European Union provide for the measures taken to resolve the BSE crisis in the United Kingdom and what percentage of the expenditure did this aid represent?

What funding is available and what percentage of the expenditure will the European Union provide to cofinance measures to assist the countries affected by this new BSE crisis?

 
  
 

The measures in aid of cattle farmers during the 1996 crisis were of two kinds: direct aid to farmers in accordance with Council Regulations (EC) Nos 1356/96 and 2443/96; and traditional measures in terms of withdrawals from the market.

I can let the honourable Member have a summary of the EAGGF’s expenditure on BSE in 1996 and 1997, totalling approximately ECU 2 150 million.

With regard to those measures that have been adopted so far, both by the Council and the Commission, in response to the BSE crisis, the Council would refer the honourable Member to the answer already given to the question from John McCartin (H-60/01) at the February part-session, a copy of which will be sent to her as soon as possible by the Council.

At present, a number of measures, on which the European Parliament is called upon to give its opinions, are being put to the Council. It is therefore too early to say what type of measures the Council will finally make up its mind to adopt in response to the current BSE crisis.

 

Question no 29 by Carmen Cerdeira Morterero (H-0198/01)
 Subject: El Ejido
 

In its resolution of 17 February 2000 concerning the incidents which occurred at El Ejido on 5 to 7 February 2000, the European Parliament welcomed the agreement reached by all the parties concerned (Spanish Government, Andalusia regional government, El Ejido local authorities, businessmen, trade unions, immigrants' associations, etc.) concerning a series of measures to resolve the problem of providing assistance for health, education and housing.

Will the Council examine the causes and practical effects of these incidents and the measures adopted, to ensure that events of this kind, which run counter to the values represented by the European Union, do not recur?

 
 

Question no 30 by Glyn Ford (H-0202/01)
 Subject: Immigration policy - El Ejido
 

The racism that flared up in El Ejido between 5 and 7 February 2000 illustrated the difficulties facing the European Union: large­scale immigration of workers, economic development and a lack of dialogue and understanding between communities. The problems have not gone away, and could trigger further crises, both in Andalusia and in other regions of Europe. Does the Council not believe that the time has now come to hold a far­reaching debate on immigration policy, addressing all aspects of the question, rather than adopting a piecemeal and frequently restrictive approach towards the issues involved?

 Subject: Follow-up to the events in El Ejido
 

What does the Council intend to do to ensure that the housing conditions of a large number of horticultural workers in El Ejido in Andalusia respect the provisions of the Charter of Fundamental Rights and do not constitute an affront to human dignity?

 
 

Question no 32 by Pedro Marset Campos (H-0215/01)
 Subject: Immigrant workers in El Ejido
 

What steps does the Council intend to take to ensure that immigration is handled more humanely, particularly as regards sectors employing large number of immigrant workers, such as the horticultural industry in El Ejido in Andalusia, so as to facilitate the integration of these communities?

 
 

Question no 33 by Fodé Sylla (H-0217/01)
 Subject: El Ejido: why have there been no prosecutions?
 

On 5-7 February 2000, extremely serious incidents occurred in El Ejido in Andalusia. Both large numbers of eye witnesses and the media stated that the riots were racist and xenophobic in nature, and the European Parliament adopted a resolution severely censuring what had taken place. What does the Council intend to do in view of the fact that there have been no prosecutions or sentences in connection with the events at El Ejido a year ago, although almost 700 complaints have been lodged?

Joint Answer

1. The honourable Members should note that extensive and intensive debates are currently being conducted within the Council’s different bodies on a number of issues affecting immigration policy within the framework of an overarching strategy.

2. It may be remembered that Article 63 of the Treaty establishing the European Community provides for the Council to decide upon measures relating to policy on both legal and illegal immigration.

The European Council in Tampere called for a common EU policy on immigration to be developed and emphasised the need for an overarching strategy on all problems and issues affecting immigration. The Tampere conclusions gave priority not only to the fight against illegal immigration but also to an integration policy granting third country citizens legal status with appropriate rights and guarantees. The need to enter into partnership with immigrants’ countries of origin was also emphasised.

3. So as to be able to implement the regulations in Article 63 of the Treaty, the Commission has, as a result of the Tampere conclusions, designed a ‘scoreboard’ for monitoring progress in creating an ‘area of freedom, security and justice’ in the European Union. The scoreboard provides a detailed framework and a timetable for measures which will deal with all the relevant problems outlined in the Tampere conclusions in terms of a coordinated strategy. The Commission is also considering developing a new immigration strategy and establishing a framework for the Community’s immigration policy. A communication was therefore submitted recently to the Council, with a long-term strategy for dealing with immigration that requires a large number of effective measures to be taken both by the Community and the Member States.

4. On the basis of the scoreboard, the Commission has already presented the Council with various proposals for legislative measures, and these are at present being discussed both at technical and political levels. The Commission will shortly be tabling further proposals which will be announced on the scoreboard.

5. With regard to partnership with immigrants’ countries of origin, the work done by the high-level group for asylum and immigration is of the greatest importance. The high-level group developed an integrated strategy, not confined to any one pillar, for tackling the basic reasons why people migrate or become refugees and drew up action plans for certain countries from which asylum seekers and/or illegal immigrants come, including Morocco. Within the framework for implementing this plan in the course of the year 2000, two measures are of special relevance to the situation of migrant workers in Spain. These measures, comprising the following, are described in a document that was presented to the European Council in Nice.

– Programmes to assist the integration of Moroccan immigrants in Spain, for which the Spanish authorities have earmarked a total of EUR 4 373 589.

– An agreement between ministers of health and social affairs from Spain and Morocco concerning Moroccan seasonal workers in Spain.

6. The Council also emphasises that the Charter of Fundamental Rights is applicable to the Community institutions and the Member States only when they apply Community law, which is not the case in the current situation. However, the Council has resources available to it, such as the programme for combating social exclusion, and, through these, it can intervene to improve situations.

7. Finally, the Council would ask the honourable Members to note that the events in El Ejido, the prosecution of those responsible for the criminal acts committed under these circumstances, and decisions concerning preventive measures are issues which fall within the competence of the Member State concerned.

 
 

Question no 34 by Nelly Maes (H-0211/01)
 Subject: Turkey and organised trafficking in humans
 

First there was the Professor Kolesnikov with 877 people on board, then the Kalsit with 418 and the Nilderya with 533. Now there is the stranded East Sea with 908 Kurdish refugees on board. For as long as Kurds are not safe from Turkish troops destroying entire villages on Iraqi territory, for as long as the Turkish government denies the Kurds any rights on its own territory and continues to persecute and expel the Kurdish people, for as long as the threat of war in the Middle East increases the Iraqi Kurds' fear of further genocide, many Kurdish people will sell their last remaining possessions in order to pay the traffickers who will unceremoniously dump them on the shores of Europe.

Is the Council conducting an inquiry into where the ship came from and with whose cooperation it left port? Does the Council acknowledge that denying the Kurds their rights is the root cause of the problem of Kurdish refugees? Does the Council intend to exert more pressure on Turkey and Iraq?

 
  
 

Illegal immigration towards the European Union is one of the main issues the Council is dealing with, as shown by the conclusions of the European Councils in Tampere, Santa Maria da Feira and Nice. As the honourable Member will be aware, the European Council committed the European Union to intensified cooperation to combat trafficking in human beings, following the tragic events in Dover last year. However, regarding the first question, it is not for the Council to conduct such an inquiry. This matter falls within the exclusive competence of the authorities of each Member State. The Council is not authorised to take a position on a matter which does not fall within the scope of the powers conferred upon it by the Treaties.

The Council is concerned about the difficult situation for Kurds in many of their countries of origin. But there are many reasons why people, including Kurds, are fleeing to Europe from their countries of origin, including poverty, economic hardship or simply a natural desire for a better life, as well as political persecution. The EU is doing what it can to promote conditions which will reduce these pressures both politically and economically. The Accession Partnership with Turkey has a number of priorities and intermediate objectives with specific relevance for Turkish citizens of Kurdish origin. The EU strongly supports the Peace Process in the Middle East. It welcomes the fact that under UNSCRs a significant proportion of the oil-for-food programme is used in the Kurdish parts of Iraq. The Council also underlines the importance of the Action Plan for Iraq on asylum and migration adopted by the Tampere European Council. The Council is currently working on the implementation of the Action Plan, which includes, among other measures, a dialogue with Turkey on questions concerning migration.

 

Question no 35 by Astrid Thors (H-0212/01)
 Subject: Construction of port at Primorsk, Russia
 

The Russian authorities have begun construction of a large port at Primorsk in the Gulf of Finland where ice conditions are evidently the most difficult in the entire Gulf. Oil is reportedly to be exported from Primorsk, which necessitates drawing oil pipelines through areas of significance for water supplies to St Petersburg. It is also claimed that there are plans to import spent nuclear fuel/nuclear waste into Russia through this port.

What has the Swedish Presidency done and what does it intend to do to guarantee that the planned port does not result in environmental disasters in the Gulf of Finland?

 
  
 

The Council is fully aware of the environmental problems caused by the plans for expanding the port of Primorsk, by the construction of a pipeline to that port and by the fact that tankers will be navigating the Gulf of Finland. The matter was therefore discussed under "other business" by the Council at its meetings of 10 October 2000 and 8 March 2001 (Environment).

The Community has raised the matter on different occasions in the relevant bodies under the EU-Russia Partnership and Cooperation Agreement, including at the EU-Russia summit on 30 October 2000. Each time, the Community expressed its concern and suggested establishing, in the framework of the Espoo Convention on Environmental Impact Assessment in a Transboundary Context, an international panel that would evaluate the trans-border impact of the extensions of the port. The Council was informed that the Russian authorities have undertaken an environmental impact assessment and will take measures to preserve the environment around Primorsk and to protect the approaches to that port against oil spills. In particular, they informed the Finnish authorities that they intend to protect the maritime approaches to Primorsk by a strict implementation of the Marpol convention, i.a. by imposing the anticipated use of double hull tankers, and that they would in addition consider carefully the plans for constructing the oil terminal and the pipeline to St. Petersburg.

The matter is being kept under review.

 

Question no 36 by Inger Schörling (H-0222/01)
 Subject: Compatibility of the common asylum and immigration policy with the principles of the rule of law
 

All the statistics show that it is becoming increasingly difficult for refugees to enter the EU. The extension of transporters' responsibility introduced during the French Presidency is simply another expression of this tendency. Airlines, which already have to pay for a return ticket for refugees who do not have valid travel documents, are now to be subject to even higher fines. This means that it will become more difficult for refugees to enter the EU legally to seek asylum and plays into the hands of the people smugglers.

Is it reasonable to expect refugees fleeing from countries which are guilty of massive violations of human rights to have 'their papers in order'? Does the Council not see a risk that the extension of transporters' responsibility may be playing even more into the hands of people smugglers by making the conditions for entering the EU legally even stricter? Does the Council consider that increasing transporters' responsibility is compatible with the spirit of the Geneva Convention and the principles of the rule of law?

 
  
 

The proposal to which the honourable Member refers is an initiative of the French Republic with a view to the adoption of a Council Directive on the harmonisation of financial penalties imposed on carriers transporting into the territory of the Member States third-country nationals lacking the documents necessary for admission. (1)

This text, which was forwarded to the European Parliament for consultation on 6 September 2000, is at present being examined by the competent Council bodies.

The initiative draws on the will to cooperate in implementing a coherent policy for combating illegal immigration at European level. Such a policy cannot be restricted to taking measures that apply solely to illegal immigrants. Action must also and especially be directed effectively against the vectors and means that accompany, or even encourage, the phenomenon. Making carriers answerable is an essential part of this approach.

The implementation of this policy must, however, involve a shared attitude on the part of Member States, since it is essential that the European Union present a united front in its struggle against illegal immigration. A common approach is also required for the system to be effective. Divergent situations in the various Member States could lead to distortions of competition in particular in the field of air transport.

In that light, the essential aim of the initiative is to harmonise the penalties applicable to carriers as provided for in Article 26 of the Convention implementing the Schengen Agreement.

I would draw the attention of the honourable Member to the fact that according to Article 26(2) of that Convention and recital No 3 of the French initiative, ‘the draft Directive is without prejudice to the obligations resulting from the Geneva Convention of 28 June 1951 on the status of refugees, as amended by the New York Protocol of 31 January 1967.’

As recognised by Heads of State and Governments at the Tampere European Council a future Community asylum and immigration policy must be based on the full and integral application of the Geneva Convention, on rules governing the orderly admission and integration of legally arriving third country nationals, on measures aimed at combating illegal immigration, as well as on partnership with countries of origin of asylum seekers and illegal migrants. The French initiative falls under the third of these four guiding principles.

Finally, I would point out that the competent administrations of the Member States regularly train employees of transport companies in the detection of false papers and that it must be remembered that any person who wishes to enter another State must first pass through the passport control organised by the border police of that State.

 
 

(1) OJ C 269, 20.09.2000, p.8

 

Question no 37 by Per Gahrton (H-0225/01)
 Subject: What is the Swedish Presidency's objection to the German language?
 

According to reports in the German press the Swedish Presidency has decided that German will not have the same status as English and French at a number of EU meetings. This has already resulted in German and Austrian representatives stating that they will not be attending the meetings in question.

Only sixty years ago, German was the first foreign language in Swedish schools. Even today, German is much more common amongst visitors to Sweden than French. German, in fact, is the most widely spoken mother tongue in the EU.

As a former student of German I am therefore asking, in German, why the Swedish Presidency is discriminating against the German language? What is the Swedish government's objection to German?

 
  
 

The Council never comments upon information contained in national press releases.

The Council reminds the honourable Member that in 1958 the Community determined the languages to be used by the European Community in Regulation No 1, which includes, among other languages, German. The Council also recalls Article 21 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights that prohibits any discrimination based, in particular, on language.

As the honourable Member does not specify the meetings referred to (e.g. formal or informal meetings), it is difficult to give a precise answer to the question. However, in general terms, it is certainly not the intention of the Presidency to discriminate among Member States on the basis of language. It may be necessary for a particular meeting to retain interpretation into and out of a reduced number of languages for reasons of efficiency, urgency or cost.

 

Question no 38 by Konstantinos Alyssandrakis (H-0228/01)
 Subject: Annexation of Yugoslav territories
 

An article in the newspaper ‘Neues Deutschland’, which has been reproduced in the Greek press, mentions the existence of a secret plan involving the annexation by Albania of certain regions of southern Serbia, Montenegro and the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia. The article also refers to a 360-page report allegedly drawn up by a team of 30 experts at the request of the Secretary-General of NATO and the EU’s foreign policy services. Not only does the report fail to rule out the possibility of supporting the positions advocated by nationalist circles in Tirana who favour a Greater Albania, it even proposes solutions that would involve this approach, a policy which would enable NATO to remain in the region for decades to come and would better serve Western interests by providing them with military protection.

Will the Council say if there is any truth in the abovementioned article? Did EU experts take part in the work of the group which supposedly drew up the plan? If such a plan exists, what exactly does it entail?

 
  
 

The Council is not aware of any "secret plan involving the annexation by Albania of certain regions of Southern Serbia, Montenegro and the Former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia" nor of any report of 360 pages or any other length of the sort referred to by the honourable Member.

The Council, for its part, has repeatedly stated, most recently at the Zagreb Summit on 24 November, the need for countries of the region - within present borders - to cooperate and develop good neighbourly relations.

The EU excludes the hypothesis of a "Greater Albania".

 

Question no 39 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0232/01)
 Subject: Attack on Iraq
 

Peace-loving men and women all over the world have greeted the news of the barbarous and unheralded air attack by the USA and the UK on Iraq on 17 February 2001 which killed and injured many civilians, including innocent children, with outrage and exasperation. This fresh attack which was provocatively called a 'routine' operation is one more crime against the Iraqi people who are still suffering the consequences of the Gulf War and the inhuman embargo imposed on the country. It is also a blatant violation of the rules of international law and is the first sign of a new escalation in moves to destabilise the sensitive Middle East region.

Why has the Council so far failed to adopt a position on this new crime, especially given that one of the perpetrators is a Member State of the EU? What specific measures does it intend to take to lift the sanctions against Iraq and to put a definitive end to the attacks?

 
  
 

As is well known, American and British aircraft attacked military targets outside Baghdad on 16 February. The reason given for the attack was an increase in Iraqi anti-aircraft fire directed at the British and US warplanes patrolling the "no­fly" zones in northern and southern Iraq. The United States and United Kingdom have made statements stressing that the action was taken by way of self-defence, was of a routine nature and should not be regarded as an escalation or change of policy. Several countries have criticised the action taken. The Security Council held a brief discussion of the matter during informal consultations on 20 February.

As was also noted earlier by the Presidency during a debate at the European Parliament on 1 March of this year, there are differing opinions within the Union concerning the basis in international law for such military actions, including those in the no-fly zones. One Member State participated in the strikes. Other Member States have deplored what happened, but the Common Foreign and Security Policy depends on the existence of a common position and, as you all know, that is not the case here.

As for the sanctions, Security Council Resolution 1284 of December 1999 allows for the possibility of suspending them provided the Iraqi Government cooperates fully with the UN inspectors responsible for disarmament in Iraq. It is encouraging in this connection to note that a dialogue has been initiated in New York between the Secretary-General and the Iraqi Government. Naturally, we hope that these discussions can help break the deadlock and that Iraq will decide to cooperate with the UN and the UN’s weapons inspectors on the basis of the current Security Council Resolutions.

 

Question no 40 by Ioannis Patakis (H-0233/01)
 Subject: US pressure on the EU to admit Turkey
 

Only a few days after the adoption by the European Parliament in plenary sitting of the regulation on EU financial aid to Turkey(1), the former Commissioner Hans van den Broek revealed in a talk in Nicosia that the USA was exerting pressure on the EU to give Turkey preferential treatment to speed up Turkish accession. The former Commissioner stated, inter alia, that the US was asking the EU to adopt a specific approach to Turkey which 'was not necessarily compatible with the conditions which a candidate country has to fulfil…' and that 'the Americans had also approached the European Parliament, requesting it not to block the Customs Union agreement between Turkey and the EU'.

Will the Council answer the questions raised by the above declarations concerning US lobbying of the EU during the entire process of Turkish accession to the EU so far?

 
  
 

The Council has no information about any formal lobbying by the United States as regards Turkey's accession to the European Union.

As the honourable Member will already know, the Helsinki European Council reaffirmed the inclusive nature of the accession process, which now comprises 13 candidate countries, including Turkey, within a single framework. The candidate countries are participating in the accession process on an equal footing. They must share the values and objectives of the European Union as set out in the Treaties. Furthermore, compliance with the political criteria laid down at the Copenhagen European Council is a prerequisite for the opening of accession negotiations.

As the honourable Member will also know, the Helsinki European Council also established a tailor-made pre-accession strategy for Turkey. Work on this pre-accession strategy is now ongoing. In particular, the Council formally decided on the contents of the Accession Partnership on 8 March 2001. Turkey will respond to the Accession Partnership in its National Programme for the Adoption of the Acquis (NPAA).

As former Commissioner van den Broek's statement regarding the EC-Turkey Customs Union agreement concerns the European Parliament, the Council cannot take a position on this matter.

 
 

(1) Texts adopted on 14.2.2001

 

Question no 41 by Marco Pannella (H-0234/01)
 Subject: Resolution on human rights in China at the Commission on Human Rights session in Geneva
 

The United States announced yesterday that it would be tabling a resolution on human rights in the People's Republic of China at the forthcoming session of the Commission on Human Rights in Geneva.

All observers agree that the human rights situation in the People's Republic of China has considerably deteriorated over the last few years. With only a few days to go until the opening of this year's session of the Commission on Human Rights in Geneva, does the Council not consider that it ought, as a matter of urgency, to state the EU's position publicly on the tabling of a resolution condemning the grave and persistent human rights violations in China? In other words, will the Council clarify whether the Union intends to table a resolution - as the European Parliament has urged it to do on several occasions - or support the US resolution?

 
  
 

The Council will discuss the human rights situation in China, including the EU-China Human rights dialogue and the US decision to table a resolution on China at CHR, at its meeting on 19 and 20 March 2001. After the discussion, I assume the Council will clarify its position as to the forthcoming session of the Commission on Human Rights in Geneva.

In January, the Council adopted conclusions on its review of the EU-China Human Rights dialogue and recommended measures which will make the dialogue more effective, results-oriented and transparent. The Union considers that the human rights dialogue is an acceptable option only if enough progress is achieved and reflected on the ground. Therefore the results of the dialogue will continue to be assessed on a regular basis.

 Subject: Citizens' rights during summit meetings
 

According to the Swedish media, inhabitants of Gothenburg living close to where the European Summit is to be held have had their names placed on a register by the police and have been asked to give details of any guests they are planning to have around the time of, or during, the summit.

The honourable Member’s question falls within the competence of each Member State concerned.

 

Question no 43 by Ioannis Souladakis (H-0237/01)
 Subject: Economic crisis in Turkey
 

Despite the recent attempt by the IMF to save the Turkish economy by lending USD 11.4 billion, the Turkish lira has collapsed, making severe inroads into Turkey's foreign currency reserves, the stock market has plummeted and inflation and interest rates have risen sharply. This has revealed the underlying causes of the crisis facing Turkey with which the Council is familiar: excessive state interventionism, the over-indebtedness of banks, money laundering, interventions by the political and military circles in the Turkish economy, the absence of democratic institutions, etc.

Will the Council accept further delays in the modernisation of Turkey on the grounds of the currency crisis?

Will it restrict itself to the solutions imposed by the IMF or will it intervene, following moves in the USA?

How will it react if Turkey turns into a 'a new bankrupt Russia' which imposed severe strains on the economy of the EU in 1998?

What proposals does the Council have for addressing the crisis, given that the recent partnership agreement between the EU and Turkey makes no provision for specific policies in this area?

 
  
 

– The Council hopes that the financial crisis will not seriously disrupt economic development in Turkey or create hardship for the Turkish population. The crisis will not itself affect close relations between the EU and Turkey or Turkey's candidature for the EU. The EU will continue to support the Turkish reform efforts in the framework of the pre-accession strategy, including a structural adjustment facility of EUR 150 million decided upon last year which will also help to carry forward the necessary structural reforms.

– In the recently adopted Accession Partnership for Turkey, implementation of the anti-inflation and structural reform programme agreed with the IMF and the World Bank is listed among the short-term priorities. A new economic programme is being prepared by Turkey in close cooperation with the IMF, in order to replace the previous programme. The economic reforms in Turkey which the EU sees as priorities are set out in the Accession Partnership and include the "swift implementation of the financial sector reform, aiming at guaranteeing transparency and surveillance".

 

Question no 44 by Christine De Veyrac (H-0240/01)
 Subject: European heritage list
 

The Hotel Négresco in Nice, known by everyone to be one of the jewels of the French hotel trade, could vanish, as its owner of more than 40 years has no heir. If action is not taken quickly to protect the hotel, all the riches it contains could be dispersed. Emptied of its prestigious contents, this hotel and what it represents for France and for Europe, will cease to exist. The Négresco is not the only building in such a situation, for many others in Europe have already been stripped of what made them top-class historical monuments.

Could the Council act swiftly to preserve Europe's outstanding historical sites by establishing a European heritage list, as proposed in Parliament's resolution on the application of the Convention concerning the Protection of the World Cultural and Natural Heritage in the Member States of the European Union (2000/2036(INI)), adopted on 16 January 2001(1)?

The report of which this resolution forms part advocates that a European heritage list take account not only of the buildings concerned but also of the furnishings and fittings that form an integral part of them. This would make it possible to adopt a dynamic approach to heritage preservation.

What steps is the Council planning to take to ensure that such a list is drawn up as a matter of urgency at intergovernmental level, in order to protect the Négresco and the other outstanding buildings that we still have in the European Union?

 
  
 

In the cultural field, the Community measures are limited to incentive measures and recommendations (Article 151.5). Concerning these measures, the Council acts only on the basis of a Commission proposal. At the moment, the Council is not aware of any Commission proposals concerning a European heritage list.

However, the Council has consistently stressed the importance of historical continuity in architecture. Recently the Council adopted a resolution in which it encourages the Members States, among other things, to intensify their efforts to improve the knowledge and promotion of architecture and urban design.

 
 

(1) Texts adopted at the sitting of 16.1.2001.

 

Question no 45 by Olivier Dupuis (H-0244/01)
