(1) Written answer of 14.2.2001

 

Question no 46 by John Walls Cushnahan (H-0246/01)
 Subject: Elections in Sri Lanka
 

The EU sent an Electoral Observation Mission to Sri Lanka in October 2000. The Mission, which I led, produced a detailed report with a number of recommendations. What action has the Council taken on those recommendations? Furthermore, as the President of Sri Lanka is due to visit leading EU figures, will the Council be meeting her to discuss such action and the recommendations of previous Electoral Missions?

 
  
 

The EU has on several occasions raised the recommendations contained in the report of the Electoral Observation Mission to Sri Lanka, both publicly and bilaterally with the Sri Lankan authorities.

On 18 December, the EU issued a declaration in the run up of the World Bank Development Forum on Sri Lanka, where good governance was one of the key issues. The declaration recalled that the observer mission was a demonstration of the EU's support for the democratic process in Sri Lanka, regretted and condemned the anomalies observed throughout the campaign and the violence during the voting, and reminded the Government of Sri Lanka (GoSL) of the need to implement the mission's recommendations in order to guarantee greater transparency in future elections.

More recently, the issue was discussed at the EC-Sri Lanka Joint Commission, which took place in Colombo on 27/28 February 2000, within the wider discussion on improving governance in Sri Lanka. The EU was anxious to engage GoSL in constructive talks on this issue, following up on the report's recommendations and with the aim of developing an agreed programme of action which would improve the quality of governance in the country.

The GoSL assured the EU that the principal recommendations of the report had been noted and that necessary measures were either under consideration, notably in the framework of the revision of the Sri Lankan constitution which the GoSL proposed before the elections, or were already being implemented.

At this stage, the Council is not yet in a position to confirm whether the High Representative Solana will be able to meet the Sri Lankan President during her forthcoming visit in Brussels. The Council is, however, confident that the President of the Commission will raise, once again, the crucial question of improving good governance in Sri Lanka in his talks with the President Kumaratunga.

 

Questions to the Commission
Question no 62 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H-0146/01)
 Subject: Distribution of European Parliament seats in the EU 27
 

The Treaty of Nice has produced a distorted distribution of seats in the European Parliament. Greece, Belgium and Portugal with populations of 10.53 m, 10.21 m and 9.98 m respectively have been allocated 22 seats in the future parliament of the EU 27, whereas the Czech Republic and Hungary with populations of 10.29 m and 10.09 m respectively have been allocated 20 seats.

In their accession negotiations, the Czech Republic and Hungary will undoubtedly – and with every justification – demand that they should also have 22 seats in the European Parliament.

What is the Commission's position on such a demand for justice?

 
  
 

The number of seats allocated to the different Member States, current and future, is the result of a long negotiation, which culminated, at the Nice European Council, in the Heads of State and Government of the fifteen deciding to allocate 22 MEPs to Belgium, Greece and Portugal, and 20 seats were established for Hungary and the Czech Republic. The number of seats envisaged for Hungary and the Czech Republic appears in a declaration of the Intergovernmental Conference which establishes the common position which the Member States will present at accession conferences.

The decision on that common position was taken unanimously, and within the framework of a global agreement on all the institutional questions, each Member State having made concessions with a view to achieving a result at that Intergovernmental Conference. The objective of the conference was to open the way to enlargement by means of the best possible agreement amongst the Member States given the realities and positions of some of them.

 

Question no 63 by Theresa Zabell (H-0205/01)
 Subject: Legal basis for sport
 

Although the Declaration on sport adopted in Nice constitutes a significant breakthrough, its scope is limited by the fact that it does not have a legal basis.

In the Commission’s view, is the issue of sport likely to be discussed before the next Intergovernmental Conference debates a fresh distribution of powers in 2004?

When can we expect to see sport enshrined in the Treaties? Could there be major scope for establishing a specific legal basis in 2004?

 
  
 

In its declaration on the future of the Union annexed to the Treaty of Nice, the Intergovernmental Conference expressed its desire for a broad and in-depth debate on the future of the European Union. This debate, encouraged by the Swedish and Belgian Presidencies, in cooperation with the Commission and with the participation of the European Parliament, will involve all the interested parties: the representatives of the national parliaments and public opinion as a whole, including representatives of political, economic and academic fields, representatives of civil society and candidate countries, according to procedures to be defined. This process will culminate in an Intergovernmental Conference in 2004. The Commission believes that, at that conference, and also during the preparatory stages for it, the fundamental issues on the aims of the Union should be raised.

The questions we will be raising on the future of the Union will lead us to a consideration of the distribution of Community competences. It is within that framework that sport, amongst other issues, will no doubt be dealt with. However, it does not seem appropriate to approach this ‘constitutional’ debate by dealing with specific policies. We must first of all produce a coherent and sustainable plan for the enlarged Union. Once this coherent plan has been drawn up, we will be able more specifically to raise the question of the role of a particular policy and we will therefore have the opportunity to create a specific legal basis for sport.

 

Question no 64 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H-0224/01)
 Subject: Land registry in Greece
 

The implementation of the National Land Registry in Greece is still facing numerous problems (low percentage of registration of property, unsatisfactory results compared to the money spent, a lack of transparency in the procedures for awarding contracts for individual programmes and a failure to respect the original timetables for the project). Moreover, much has been written in the Greek press about the inadequacy of Greece's proposals for funding the land registry under the new CSF. What is the Commission's position as regards the assessment of the results of this programme within the framework of the second CSF? What is the outlook regarding the third CSF and what is the timescale for completing the project?

 
  
 

The ‘Establishment of the National Land Registry’ project in Greece benefits from Community aid (ERDF) to the value of around EUR 105 million under the CCA II ‘Environment’ programme.

The objective set by the programme for this project is to create an operational land registry covering an area of around 28 200 km2 by the end of 2001. Unfortunately, recent available information indicates that, while 83% of the budget has been employed, only 21% of the works have been carried out to date.

There have been delays and shortcomings in the implementation of this programme. Six years after the approval of the operational programme and despite the allocation of significant Community funds, not a single part of the project is operational. Therefore, on 19 January, the Commission sent a letter to the Greek authorities requesting clarifications and raising the possibility of applying Article 24 of Regulation (EEC) 4253 of 19 December 1988, which lays down that, in the event of proven irregularities, Community aid can be reduced or even cancelled.

The Commission will not take a decision until it has made an in-depth analysis of the Greek authorities’ response.

In the current circumstances, the Commission has agreed with the Greek authorities that this project will not form part of the new ‘Environment 2000-2006’ Operational Programme and that the corresponding sum would be added to the national reserve, with a view to a return to the programme at the appropriate time for the co-financing of environmental projects.

 

Question no 65 by Stavros Xarchakos (H-0231/01)
 Subject: Athens Underground
 

Athens Underground has been co-financed by the EU. Greek press reports have revealed that during the tunnel work archaeological treasures of inestimable value came to light. Has the Commission been notified about the precise number and nature of the archaeological finds made during the execution of the project which it has co-financed? Does it have any official information from the Greek authorities on the destruction or loss of finds during construction work on the tunnels and the stations of Athens Underground?

 
  
 

The Greek authorities have informed the Commission that the total number of archaeological findings related to the Athens Metro construction works exceeds 50.000 articles. This makes that the Athens Metro is the most important archaeological excavation ever done in Greece. The Ministry of Culture has not yet completed the classification of these findings.

The most important of them, whose number is about 500, are currently in display at the Museum of Cycladic art in Athens.

The Ministry of Culture has supervised all the excavation works done by archaeologists during the construction of the Athens Metro project. On the basis of information provided by Attiko Metro (project owner of Athens Metro), the Ministry of Culture confirms that not a single archaeological finding has been damaged during the construction works.

 

Question no 66 by Christine De Veyrac (H-0241/01)
 Subject: Franco-Spanish cross-border cooperation
 

Since 1989, the Franco-Spanish border area has been in receipt of funding from the INTERREG Programme for promoting integrated regional development between border regions.

For the 1994-1999 programming period, even before the operating programme had been adapted by the Commission, many of the Spanish partners had already allocated the full amount of the appropriations to specific projects. At that stage, the French partners were seeking to set up Franco-Spanish cross-cross-border structures to select genuinely cross-border projects.

For the current 2000-2006 programming, how does the Commission intend to guarantee that the projects funded are genuinely cross-border projects? With regard to facilitating cross-border within the European Union, when will the Commission be in a position to put forward plans for a European legal structure for cross-border cooperation?

 
  
 

The Commission acknowledges that certain weaknesses have been identified in relation to the programming periods previous to 2000-2006 with regard to the implementation of the Community INTERREG initiative on the Franco-Spanish border. The difficulties arose essentially as a result of the absence of common programming and management methods for these Structural Fund actions.

For the 2000-2006 programming period, within its Guidelines on Interreg III, the Commission has laid down that the use of new methods for the development of cross-border cooperation is mandatory. Hence, item 7 of the said Guidelines lays down that the development of a common strategy is a necessary requirement for the acceptance of projects proposed for Community funding. All specific operations selected must be of a clearly cross-border nature. The only eligible operations are therefore those which are selected and implemented jointly. Furthermore, the implementation of the cooperation must make use of common management structures.

In relation to the existence of legal structures for cross-border cooperation, the Commission’s Guidelines for Interreg 2000-2006 allow Member States the opportunity of choosing the most suitable means for promoting that cooperation. In the specific case of the Franco-Spanish border, the two Member States and all the regions involved have proposed that the region of Aquitaine should take on the role of common management authority for the new programme. The role of payment authority will be taken by the Spanish Minister for Finance. A joint technical secretariat will operate from the region of Aquitaine. Three subcommittees for the selection of projects will also operate in a joint manner.

This programme was presented to the Commission on 22 November 2000. It has been deemed admissible and negotiations are commencing between the Commission and the competent regional and national authorities. The Commission expects it to be formally approved by the summer.

 

Question no 67 by Richard Corbett (H-0135/01)
 Subject: Equal opportunities
 

Why does the Commission not consider equal opportunities in its broadest sense in the application of Structural Fund allocations (i.e. not just male/female inequalities but need to include groups of people who traditionally feel excluded, such as disabled people)?

 
  
 

The European Employment Strategy (EES) which is the cornerstone for European Social Fund (ESF) activity deals with this issue. In guideline 7, the employment guidelines encourage Member States to “implement appropriate measures to meet the needs of the disabled, ethnic minorities and migrant workers as regards there integration in to the labour market and set national targets where appropriate for this purpose”. Recently agreed ESF programmes reflect these commitments.

In reforming ESF, there was a desire to move away from the target group approach to programme development to one based on defining clearer policy themes. This aimed to move to a position of management by objectives rather than management by inputs (e.g. numbers in a target group). Consequently, one of the 5 policy fields specifically promoted through ESF focuses on “promoting equal opportunities for all in accessing the labour market, with particular emphasis on those exposed to social exclusion”.

Therefore, the Commission considers equal opportunities not only with regard to women and men but in its broadest sense in the application of Structural Funds allocations. There are a number of references to the objective of including all groups of people and combating any discrimination or social exclusion.1

The aim of the Community Initiative EQUAL, funded by the European Social Fund, is to promote new means of combating all forms of discrimination and inequalities in connection with the labour market.

In this framework, ESF programmes have made a clear commitment to supporting the needs of people with disabilities and others at risk of social exclusion. Latest estimates suggest that the ESF will contribute in the region of 9 billion Euro towards the fight against social exclusion over the period 2000-2006.

 

Question no 68 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H-0136/01)
 Subject: Passage of ships carrying radioactive materials through waters close to Member States
 

The ship Pacific Pintail recently left the French port of Cherbourg bound for Japan carrying a cargo of plutonium and uranium oxide. Does the Commission know what exact route this ship will be taking when passing through waters close to Member States? What measures are being taken to prevent pollution of Community waters in the event of an accident?

 
  
 

The regulations from the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) for the safe transport of radioactive materials, which have been implemented into Members States regulations, govern the package design in order to ensure the safety of people and the environment in extreme accident conditions, regardless of the mode of transport.

As the safety of all transportation operations lies primarily in the package, the Uranium and Plutonium oxide (MOX fuel assemblies) are transported in a specific cask, licensed by British, French and Japanese Authorities. Each cask is designed to ensure the safety of the transport.

The casks and the sea transport vessels used, as well as the organisation of the entire transport should meet the latest requirements of the applicable international and national regulations (national regulation has been established in France, United Kingdom, and Japan), which derives from the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) conventions, including those related to safety (IAEA recommendations).

It is up to the National Authorities to insure the conformity of the packages and the vessels and the compliance to the above mentioned regulations.

In addition, Council directive 93/75 EEC concerning minimum requirements for vessels bound for or leaving a port in a Member State and carrying dangerous goods constrains the operator of such vessel to notify before departure the competent authority of that Member State about the destination, the intended route and the nature of the dangerous goods transported.

The Commission has no means to monitor the sea route of the vessels.

Moreover, in the field of response in the event of an accidental pollution, reference can be made to the Decision 2850/2000 EEC of the Parliament and of the Council setting up a framework for cooperation in the field of accidental or deliberate marine pollution.

 

Question no 69 by Monica Frassoni (H-0138/01)
 Subject: Waste incineration and structural funds in Italy
 

In the context of the negotiations on the proposal submitted by the Commission for a Directive(1) on the promotion of electricity from renewable energy sources, Italy is claiming that the incineration of waste (urban and industrial) is a renewable energy source. If this idea were accepted, it would mean an increase in emissions of carbon dioxide and toxic substances and an end to the policy of reclamation, recycling and the low environmental-impact processing of organic waste (biodigestion and composting). The programmes financed under the 2000-2006 structural funds in Italy provide for measures in the area of waste management.

What is the Commission’s position on Italy’s demands in relation to waste incineration and on the Directive on renewable energy sources? Does the Commission intend to fund the building of waste incineration plants under the 2000-2006 structural funds? Does the Commission not consider it preferable for priority to be given to the reclamation and recycling of waste, including the low environmental-impact processing of organic waste (biodigestion and composting)?

 
  
 

The Commission is currently finalising its opinion on the Council’s common position on the Directive on the promotion of electricity from renewable energy sources. This opinion will also set out the Commission’s position on the provisions in the common position relating to the incineration of certain forms of waste including biodegradable urban and industrial waste.

With regard to waste management, the Community Support Framework for Italy’s Objective 1 regions stipulates that actions will focus on: the establishment of ‘ambiti territoriali ottimali’ and their management plans (a prerequisite for undertaking further actions); the promotion and staging of information campaigns for prevention and innovation; the development of a system for integrated waste management in line with Community priorities and the organisation of training actions and actions aimed at creating new businesses.

The definition of the management plans (drafted in accordance with Community Directives 91/156/EEC, 91/689/EEC and 94/62/EEC) and priorities for action must be consistent with the Community priorities in the field of waste management and must stipulate the prior analysis of the region’s needs and specific characteristics.

The Commission – in line with the Community Strategy for Waste Management as well as the Parliament Resolution and the Council Resolution on waste management in the Community – considers that ‘prevention of the generation of waste shall remain the first priority, where the generation can not be avoided, it should be reused or recovered for its material or its energy. Preference should be given, where environmentally sound, to the recovery of materials over energy recovery operations’. In other words, recycling shall take precedence over incineration. As stipulated in the above-mentioned directives, waste management plans must be reported to the Commission, which will establish whether they comply with the directives.

 
 

(1) COM(2000)0279

 

Question no 70 by Armonia Bordes (H-0139/01)
 Subject: Authorisation of mergers
 

The European Commission grants authorisations for mergers involving European companies. Its main concern is clearly to ensure that the rules on competition are applied.

Major companies which decide to merge always use mergers to get rid of thousands of jobs and to close down industrial sites.

The adverse employment and social consequences of mergers usually come to the attention of public opinion when the merger is announced and therefore cannot escape the Commission’s notice when it gives its opinion.

The job losses are particularly shocking because they occur in companies whose success and good performance are plain for all to see. Despite this, the Commission does not seem to take any account of this aspect of mergers when it grants authorisations.

Since the European Union, in all of the texts it adopts on economic policy, is in the habit of adding one or two paragraphs saying that it wishes to preserve Europe’s social dimension, and in order to ensure that these declarations do not remain just meaningless words, we should like to know how many mergers the current Commission has authorised and how many jobs have been lost as a result?

 
  
 

The worldwide trend of increasing merger activities is clearly reflected in the growing number of cases notified to and decided by the Commission. In 2000 the Commission made 345 decisions, which means an increase of 28% compared to 1999. The respective figure for 1999 was 270, which represented an 18% increase over the previous year. In 2000, 321 cases were cleared in Phase I, of which 28 were cleared conditionally; in phase II three cases were cleared unconditionally, 12 with conditions attached, and two were prohibited (six cases were withdrawn during an in-depth investigation before a final decision was taken).

It is not possible to ascertain exactly how many jobs are lost due to mergers. The figures mentioned (if any) when mergers are announced very often do not adequately reflect the actual development afterwards. Figures provided may both under- and overestimate eventual job losses. In addition, it is not always clear, whether job losses can really be attributed to mergers as such, or whether they are not rather the result of exogenous changes in market conditions and induced structural changes, which are at the same time the driving forces behind these mergers. A balanced evaluation of the effect of mergers and other restructuring on jobs would also have to take account of possible job preservation effects.

However, there can be no doubt that mergers do very often involve substantial cuts in employment, and that this presents a very severe problem, especially for those workers who are directly affected. The basic objective of the Community’s competition policy is to ensure the maintenance of competitive markets. At the same time, competition policy has important implications for employment and social policy and it seems necessary to examine the links which exist between these policies.

Within the current context it appears for example important that the views and interests of workers and their representatives are adequately reflected and heard in the process leading to the establishment of a merger. This concerns first of all effective involvement of workers' representatives within the concerned companies, through adequate and timely procedures of information and consultation as provided for in existing Community and national legislation and practice, as well as in legislation under adoption (notably the Commission's proposal on information and consultation in the EC). It also concerns the possibility for them to present their views to competent public authorities, notably on the implications of planned mergers, as provided for, e.g., in the Merger Regulation.

As announced in the Commission’s Social Policy Agenda of June last year and endorsed by the Nice European Council the Commission will continue to examine the relationship between social policy and competition policy with a view to better articulate both policies, as well as to address the employment and social consequences of economic and market integration (mergers, acquisitions).

 

Question no 71 by Patricia McKenna (H-0140/01)
 Subject: Slow processing of complaints because of a lack of staff in the Commission
 

The Commission is the European Institution responsible for compliance with the Treaty. I understand that complaints and infringement procedures forwarded to the Commission are being processed very slowly, mainly because of a lack of staff in the legal services of the various Directorates-General. In many instances, complaints lodged by European citizens are not properly processed and in some cases such complaints are only taken seriously into consideration by Commission staff after pressure from MEPs. Roughly one third of these complaints concern environmental aspects.

What is the Commission doing to make sure that these complaints are being properly processed and that EU legislation is fully implemented and respected at local and regional level?

Is the Commission planning to strengthen its legal services responsible for the follow-up of these complaints so as to speed up and improve the Commission’s supervisory obligations, with particular regard to the environment?

 
  
 

1a) Complaints are an important means of detecting cases of infringement, and the Commission has always been concerned to uphold petitioners' fundamental rights, in particular the right to information. However, we must not lose sight of the fact that the primary objective of the infringement procedure is to secure compliance by the Member State concerned. In that connection, the conclusions reached by the honourable Member cannot be applied solely to the time taken up by internal processing, which is essentially a function of the complexity of the case, but must include the time taken by Member States to comply with calls and services of notice to remedy the situation, which is directly linked to the ability and determination of Member States to put an end to the grievance.

1b) The Commission can assure the honourable Member that complaints are followed up with the greatest seriousness, and is prepared to investigate the cases to which the honourable Member refers.

2. The Commission has set up internal procedures for conducting, according to time frames and procedures that have been duly publicised, investigations into cases of infringement notified to it through complaints(1). Moreover, petitioners are kept systematically informed of the stages in and average duration of the Community procedure and of the options for seeking redress before national courts in order, where appropriate, to recover damages(2).

3. The importance of monitoring the implementation of Community law, with regard, in particular, to the environment, must be assessed as a whole(3) in the light of the objectives pursued and the priorities laid down by the Institution.

 
 

(1) Written Question E-0352/95 by Mr Bartho PRONK
(2) Judgment of 5 March 1996, Joint Cases C-46/93 and C-48/93
(3) On average, 60% of complaints relating to the environment do not identify grievances precisely and therefore cannot be pursued, 20% of situations complained of are remedied before proceedings can be opened under Article 226, and barely 10% actually give rise to the infringement procedure.

 

Question no 72 by Paul Rübig (H-0143/01)
 Subject: Mandate to CEN to prepare a harmonised standard for the safety of existing lifts similar to that for new lifts
 

In December 1998, the European Parliament organised a public hearing to collect information on action undertaken by the Member States to improve the safety of existing lifts in line with Commission Recommendation 95/216/EC(1). I was personally involved in this important event. At the time, the results proved very disappointing.

In the meantime, there are no true signs of any willingness on the part of the Member States to address this important issue for European citizens who daily use the 3 million lifts in service of the EU. Yet every user takes it as a matter of course that they are equally safe. They are not!

Indeed, a standard would offer Member States the possibility of defining an action plan to bring the safety level of existing installations progressively and selectively close to that of lifts installed under the New Lifts Directive. Such an action plan should aim at eliminating major risks in an initial phase with a view to avoiding any further serious injuries and fatalities.

Given that the Lifts Directive 95/16/EC(2) is supported by European harmonised standards (EN 81 1 & 2 and others) as part of the ‘new approach’ principle, would the Commission be ready to ask CEN to prepare a similar harmonised standard for the safety of existing lifts?

 
  
 

The Commission believes it is important that the safety of lifts already in service continues to improve, which is the aim of Commission Recommendation 95/216/EC of 8 June 1995, concerning the improvement of safety for existing lifts. It recommends that Member States take all necessary action to improve the safety of existing lifts, based on the principles given in Annex I to the Recommendation.

The public hearing organised by Parliament in December 1998 certainly helped raise awareness on this issue within the Community.

A standard on the safety of existing lifts would be very useful. In fact the European Committee for Standardisation (CEN) is currently drafting a standard on the “Rules for the improvement of safety of existing lifts”. This draft standard will soon be subject to public inquiry. It is likely to become an important tool for Member States in their efforts to take action according to the Commission Recommendation.

Parliament and Council Directive 95/16/EC of 29 June 1995, on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to lifts assures the circulation of “new” lifts in the European economic area (EEA). It would therefore not be possible to use it as a legal base for mandating a harmonised CEN standard on existing lifts.

The elaboration of standards is a responsibility of CEN and is based on priorities laid down by all involved parties, as national standardisation bodies, industries, etc. CEN being a private body, it has the possibility to elaborate all documents it deems necessary or useful to help industry and to ensure a high level of safety. In the case where there is no mandate from the Commission, such an elaborated standard can not give any presumption of conformity according to New Approach Directives but remains a very useful guidance tool for industry and user.

 
 

(1) OJ L 134, 20.6.1995, p. 37.
(2) OJ L 213, 7.9.1995, p. 1.

 

Question no 73 by Catherine Stihler (H-0151/01)
 Subject: Community guidelines on State aid to maritime transport
 

Does the revision of the Community guidelines on State aid to maritime transport (OJ C 205, 5.7.1997, p. 5) scheduled for 2002 mean that the Scottish Executive’s tendering proposals, currently before the Commission, should be delayed?

 
  
 

The Community guidelines on State aid to maritime transport have been effective since 5th July 1997, the date of their publication in the Official Journal of the European Communities. It is expected that the guidelines will be revised in 2002.

With respect to the issue of tendering in relation to the ferry services off the west coast of Scotland which are currently operated by Caledonian MacBrayne, the state aid guidelines must be seen in the light of Council Regulation 3577/92 of 7 December 19922 on the freedom to provide services to maritime transport within Member States. Article 4 of this Regulation permits public service obligations for the provision of maritime transport services, within a Member State, on shipping companies participating in regular services to, from and between islands. Whenever a Member State imposes public service obligations, it shall define it precisely and entrust it on a non-discriminatory basis in respect of all Community shipowners; it shall also limit the financial support to the extra costs incurred by the operator in providing the service. A public tender is an effective way of meeting these requirements and ensuring the market price of the services concerned. It is for this reason that the state aid guidelines specify the expectation of a public tender as one of the criteria for permitting the reimbursement of operating losses incurred as a direct result of fulfilling certain public service obligations not to have to be formally notified as State aid under Article 88(3) of the EC Treaty.

There are currently no plans to alter Article 4 of Council Regulation 3577/92. Whatever form the revision of the state aid guidelines may finally take, the principle of non-discrimination will remain and will need to be fulfilled by Member States wishing to establish public service obligations.

 

Question no 74 by Manuel Pérez Álvarez (H-0154/01)
 Subject: Prevention of accidents at sea
 

This harsh winter has seen numerous accidents at sea which have resulted in deaths, disappearances and great difficulty for those involved in these situations of emergency, shipwreck, drifting, boardings, etc.

Many of the consequences of these situations could have been avoided or mitigated if the crews had had the appropriate personal protection equipment for their work (and had been using it at the time of the incident), something that would be perfectly feasible in this day and age.

Has the Commission considered or is it considering making it compulsory for workers to use safety equipment (such as a suitable lifejacket), or encouraging its use in order to protect against the risk of drowning?

Does it intend to coordinate measures by those Member States involved in fishing activities with a view to the adoption of these safety measures?

 
  
 

The main principles of the protection of the health and safety of workers are laid down by the Framework Directive 89/391/EEC on the introduction of measures to encourage improvements in the safety and health of workers at work. These principles are fully applicable to workers on board of all kind of vessels. Article 16 of this directive establishes that individual directives should be developed to cover activities of specific risk, in particular, in the field of fisheries and agriculture.

For this reason, in 1993, the Council adopted Directive 93/103/EC concerning the minimum safety and health requirements for work on board fishing vessels.

This directive sets down the minimum safety and health requirements for new fishing vessels (in Annex I), and for existing vessels (in Annex II) including, inter alia, the minimum requirements on seaworthiness and stability, as well as on the conditions of decks, bulkheads and deckheads in order to prevent falls. In particular, Article 7(1) foresees that Member States shall take the measures necessary to ensure that, without prejudice to the skipper's responsibility, owners shall keep on board the vessel an adequate quantity of suitable equipment and survival equipment in good working order and, without prejudice to the provisions established by the Council Directive 89/656/EEC on the minimum requirements for the use by workers of personal protective equipment at the workplace, shall take account of the personal protective equipment specifications given in Annex IV to Directive 93/103/EC.

Moreover, the Commission, being extremely sensitive to the protection of falls and drowning, adopted a Communication in 1989 for the implementation of the Council Directive 89/656/EEC concerning the assessment of the safety aspects of personal protective equipment with a view to the choice and use thereof. Paragraph 8 of the Annex of this Communication describes the non-exhaustive information for evaluating life jackets for professional use as personal protective equipment. This description includes the risk, the origin and type of risk and the safety and performance criteria for selection of equipment.

In this context, the Commission considers that the above mentioned directives cover in a reasonable way the prevention of the risk of drowning. However, it should be noted that it belongs to the national authorities of the Member States the control and enforcement of the national legislation transposing the Health and Safety Directives.

 

Question no 75 by Caroline F. Jackson (H-0155/01)
 Subject: Terrestrial Trunk Radio - 'TETRA'
 

Considerable concern is being expressed in the United Kingdom at the possible damage to human health from the new Terrestrial Trunk Radio ('TETRA') system, due to come into use very shortly. It is argued that the lower frequency used will interfere with brain-wave activity and increase the long-term risk of cancer. Can the Commission state what consideration was given to these health concerns when the TETRA standard was devised in the 1990s and whether it agrees that the current public concerns now need to be readdressed?

 
  
 

The honourable Member raises the important issue of how public health and new technologies interact as regards in particular the expanding use of telecommunication systems.

A European protection framework is already in place in form of the Council Recommendation of 1999 on the limitation of exposure of the general public to electromagnetic fields. The Terrestrial Trunk Radio (TETRA) system, which the honourable Member mentions, was already known and taken into account during the preparation of the Recommendation, and the system is covered by the scope of the Recommendation. The exposure limits of the Recommendation are not based on the effects of any particular system, but on the guidelines regarding protection against health effects from exposure to electromagnetic fields developed by the International Commission on Non-Ionising Radiation Protection. These guidelines, which include specific exposure limits regarding the TETRA system, were endorsed by the Commission’s Scientific Steering Committee, which stated that these international guidelines provide the appropriate basis to develop exposure limits against public health risks.

In order to cover any new scientific findings and to ensure that the scientific basis, on which the Council Recommendation is based, is still valid, the Scientific Steering Committee has been asked to give an up-dated opinion in June 2001.

To ensure a uniform level of protection throughout the Community the Commission has mandated the standardisation bodies (CENELEC and ETSI) to work on establishing European standards, including harmonised methods of calculation and measurement. This will enable verification that the safe exposure limits set in the Recommendation are met. A first standard for mobile telephones is expected to be available in June 2001, and other standards will follow.
