Question no 77 by Roy Perry (H-0221/01)
 Subject: Delegation to the Arabian Gulf
 

Given the high level of EU exports to the countries of the Gulf Cooperation Council (14,573 million euro in 2000, the fifth highest export market for the EU in the world) and given the importance of the Arabian Gulf in political and strategic terms, can the Commission explain the absence of a permanent accredited Delegation anywhere in the Gulf? Has it any plans to remedy this glaring deficiency?

Joint Answer

The Commission is aware of the importance of opening a Delegation in Riyadh.

In 1998, the Commission included Saudi Arabia in its list of priorities for the opening of new Delegations and Offices. But, the Commission does not at present have sufficient resources - financial and human - to enable it to open all the Offices it would like.

The overall structure of the External Service is currently under review, not least to take account of the new needs arising from the reform and deconcentration of external assistance. The emphasis must be on consolidating the existing network of delegations.

The Commission cannot therefore give any undertaking as to when it might open a Delegation in Saudi Arabia.

 
 

Question no 78 by Marianne Eriksson (H-0158/01)
 Subject: Depleted uranium
 

Some EU Member States already have stockpiles of depleted uranium weapons. Military crisis management will give the European Union access to NATO's material resources. What steps is the Commission planning to take to ensure that depleted uranium is not used in future EU operations?

 
  
 

This is a matter for the Council; it does not fall within the competence of the European Commission.

 

Question no 79 by Ursula Schleicher (H-0163/01)
 Subject: Russia's use of energy suppliers as a foreign policy tool
 

The Russian Government has cut off gas supplies to Georgia by Intergazstroi (see report in New York Herald Tribune, 9 January 200l). What implications does this development have for the security of oil and gas supplies from Russia to the Member States?

 
  
 

The Union has already expressed its concerns to the Russian Federation about recent interruptions of gas supplies from Russia to Georgia. As regards deliveries of oil and gas from the Russian Federation to the Member States, these are subject to long term contracts, and so far all parties concerned have reliably met their contractual obligations.

Improving the Community’s energy security is a key objective of the dialogue between the Union and Russia agreed at the Union-Russia Summit in Paris last October.

 

Question no 80 by Camilo Nogueira Román (H-0165/01)
 Subject: Plans to restructure the Commission's Directorate-General for Fisheries
 

Unofficial information and the evasive replies given by the Director-General for Fisheries at the relevant European Parliament committee meeting on 5 February last, indicate that the Commission is planning to restructure the Directorate-General, dividing responsibility for international agreements, and bringing all the northern agreements with Norway, Greenland and the Baltic countries, Structural Fund policy for fisheries and policy on quotas and TAC’s in Community waters under a single directorate, with the southern international agreements to be treated differently and positively marginalised.

As a representative of Galiza, a country whose dependence on fisheries could hardly be greater, I am worried that these decisions will strengthen the interests of the EU’s northern Member States to the detriment of the south, and will favour an approach to fisheries policy in which those decisive international aspects basically located in the south or in the western Atlantic and other oceans will be treated with contempt and relegated to a subordinate role. What conceivable reason can there be for the Commission to take such an ill-advised decision?

 
  
 

The reorganisation of the Directorate-General for Fisheries is taking place in accordance with the decision-making procedures in force within the Commission. The detailed organisational structure will not be published until the end of this process.

In relation to the defence of Member States’ interests – whatever they may be – with respect to third countries, the Commission organises its activities in such a way as to respond effectively according to its available resources. The same is true of the Directorate-General for Fisheries.

 

Question no 81 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H-0167/01)
 Subject: Prospects for women in Iran
 

What progress has been made in Iran over the past four years as regards improving women's living conditions, rights and freedoms? What projects will be carried out to enhance women's social status? Will the 'trade liberalisation' for which preparations are now being made be accompanied by a 'personal liberalisation' that will include women?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware that the situation for women in Iran leaves a lot to be desired. In the past four years, since the election of President Khatami, hope for reform in this area as well as others has grown. Unfortunately, the reformers in the elected bodies have difficulties in implementing their policies. (For instance, the proposal to raise the age of marriage for girls from 9 to 14 is only slowly making its way through the decision-making bodies.)

The lack of progress on reform and the backlash in the areas of freedom of the press and freedom of expression give rise to concern.

The Commission has tried to take both sides of this picture into account in its Communication to the Council on the relations with Iran. Opening up of trade and economic cooperation with Iran will be set against the progress of reform in Iranian society, including the status of women and human rights.

 

Question no 82 by Gerard Collins (H-0169/01)
 Subject: Ensuring new technologies for rural areas
 

Will the Commission outline the measures it is taking to ensure that the benefits of new and evolving technologies are distributed equally between rural and urban areas?

 
  
 

The Commission shares the honourable Member's interest to ensure that new information and communication technologies benefit both rural and urban areas within the Community. The Commission recognises that such technologies can present an important opportunity for the economic and social development of rural areas, by overcoming features such as distance and isolation, which have previously constrained their development. However, to realise this potential, it is also clear that rural areas and communities need the same level of access to these technologies, and under similar conditions, to users of these technologies in urban centres.

The new Structural Funds regulations for the period 2000-2006 provide the scope for financing actions in this field. Indeed, the information society has been identified in Commission Guidelines for the period 2000-2006 as one of the priorities to be implemented. That priority has been converted into practice as a feature of most Objective 1 and 2 programmes. The choice of measures within these programmes is then the responsibility of the Member States and regions.

Moreover, the second instalment of intervention under the heading of innovative actions in regional policy includes financing for programmes to promote the information society at the service of regional development. This applies to all regions, rural or urban, that are eligible in whole or in part for Objectives 1 and 2.

In recognition of their interest for rural areas, the Commission identified the use of new technologies to make the products and services of rural areas more competitive as one of the four priority themes for the Community Initiative for rural development for the period 2000-2006, LEADER+.

 

Question no 83 by Niall Andrews (H-0171/01)
 Subject: Commission response to the humanitarian crisis in Afghanistan
 

As the Commission is aware, reports from aid workers in Afghanistan reflect an alarming picture of humanitarian suffering on an increasingly large scale, with UN sanctions contributing to the situation by disrupting the distribution of relief supplies. What is the Commission’s current assessment of the situation, will it say what it considers needs to be done and can be done, and does it agree that the UN sanctions should be lifted?

 
  
 

The Commission is, of course, very aware of the desperate humanitarian situation which exists today in many parts of Afghanistan.

Winters are particularly harsh in Afghanistan, made worse this year by the continuation during the winter of the ongoing conflict and by the prolonged effects of the worst drought in the country for 30 years.

International donors have reacted to this crisis with increased shipments of food aid and medical supplies, as well as continuing with support for activities in the field by United Nations agencies, international aid agencies and NGOs.

Except for a brief period following the introduction of the latest UN sanctions the activities of these aid agencies and non-governmental organisations (NGOs) have continued as previously.

The Commission’s own activities through the UN and others on behalf of vulnerable groups of internationally displaced persons (IDPs) and Afghan refugees are also continuing.

The Union’s position on the situation in Afghanistan is clear and is set out in the latest Common Position on Afghanistan dated 22 January 2001.

In this Common Position, which of course the Commission fully supports, the Union sets out its principle objectives as being the bringing about of a lasting peace, the ending of foreign intervention and the encouragement of an inter-Afghan dialogue through the UN.

The Union applies a set of sanctions, which were decided by the UN Security Council under Chapter VII of the UN Charter and are therefore mandatory for UN members. In addition, it continues to apply its own embargo on the export of arms, munitions and military equipment to those parts of Afghanistan that are not controlled by the Taliban and urges other countries to adopt a similar policy.

Some days ago, the restrictive measures against the Taliban set out in UNSCR 1333 of 19 December 2000 have been adopted by the Council following discussions between the Member States and the Commission. Any decision on the lifting of these sanctions will have to be compatible with the obligations of Member States under the UN Charter.

 

Question no 84 by Brian Crowley (H-0173/01)
 Subject: Reducing health gaps and the applicant countries
 

On 7 October 2000, the European Anti-Poverty Network adopted a resolution on healthcare for the disadvantaged, demanding that reducing health gaps should be a condition of membership of the Union and that the requirements set out in its resolution, which ranged from guaranteed access to all forms of healthcare to an enlarged role for NGOs should be applied to the applicant countries. What is the Commission’s response to this resolution?

 
  
 

The European Anti-Poverty Network (EAPN) resolution that is mentioned by the honourable Member firstly calls for European guidelines to improve access to healthcare for all and strongly argues for the need to develop an integrated approach to health issues within social inclusion. The Commission considers that the open method of coordination that has just started in the field of social inclusion provides the appropriate framework for addressing these EAPN concerns, keeping in mind the limits of Community competencies in the area of health.

In accordance with Article 152 of the EC Treaty, Community action in the field of health complements and supports that of Member States, by promoting cooperation among them. It shall fully respect their responsibilities in the organisation and delivery of health services and medical care. Recent initiatives in this respect includes a Communication on the health strategy of the Community, and a proposal for a new programme of action in the field of public health (COM (2000) 285 final of 16.5.2000). The communication identifies wide variations and inequalities in health status among the serious public health problems that cannot be ignored. The proposal for a new programme, which is currently being discussed by Parliament and the Council, contains a key action on developing strategies and measures on socio-economic health determinants. The purpose is inter alia to develop a methodology for benchmarking and linking strategies to identify health inequalities, and to review and identify obstacles to access to health services across internal borders in the Community.

Concerning the need to promote equal access to health as a part of efforts for promoting social inclusion, the Nice European Council adopted a set of common objectives for policies against social exclusion and poverty, among which it is mentioned the need for "policies which aim to provide access for all to healthcare appropriate to their situation, including situations of dependency". The Commission is committed to ensuring that this priority is properly reflected in the national action plans to combat social exclusion that Member States will submit by 1st June 2001

The EAPN resolution also calls for universal and equal access to good quality social protection, including health care, to be a condition of membership of the Community for candidate countries.

Membership of the Community requires adoption and full implementation by a candidate country of the acquis communautaire. In the area of public health this covers essentially Community programmes and tobacco directives. In this context, the Council Recommendation 92/442/EEC on the Convergence of Social Protection Objectives and Policies sets out a range of principles to guide the development of social protection systems, including health care. From the starting of the negotiation process the Community has recalled the importance it attaches to assuring a high level of human health protection in the definition and implementation of all Community policies.

Furthermore, the candidates countries are invited to develop a health monitoring system in order to obtain health data and indicators comparable with the Community health monitoring system. At the moment all the candidate countries, with the exception of Bulgaria, Romania and Turkey, have accepted the acquis. This is already implemented in their legal system, or will be implemented before the accession. The Commission continues to monitor this closely.

 

Question no 85 by James (Jim) Fitzsimons (H-0175/01)
 Subject: Sustainable development and SMUs
 

Recent research carried out by the European Foundation for the Improvement of Living and Working Conditions has highlighted the fact that economic efficiency, environmental protection and workplace safety are three key policy areas of sustainable development. It has also shown that the cost of learning about the environment and health and safety is proportionately greater for SMUs than for larger companies and has suggested that informal local networks can reduce the costs of disseminating good practice. How does the Commission intend to follow up this research, with particular regard to helping SMUs to deal with these issues on the same basis as large companies?

 
  
 

The objective of the sustainable development policy is to ensure that enterprise policies, environmental policies and social policies are conducted consistently. In particular, in their implementation, they must take into account any impact they may have on each other, in order, for example, to ensure that the implementation of environmental measures will have the lowest possible impact on the international competitiveness of European enterprises.

The implementation of these policies can lead to the introduction of new legislation or legal obligations and thus place constraints on undertakings, in particular in the areas of the environment, health or safety, as demonstrated by the research conducted by the European Foundation for the Improvement of Living and Working Conditions.

In general, such constraints will be proportionally greater for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs). Taking size into account, the cost of learning about the regulatory framework (access to information, acting on that information, making adjustments for the particular circumstances of the enterprise, etc) is, of course, relatively higher for SMEs.

As a means of reducing the cost to SMEs, the proximity networks solution has been considered to be one of the most effective, and the operation of enterprise support networks such as Euro Info Centres (EICs) is a response to exactly that concern.

Those networks were set up for the purpose of providing, at local level and close to enterprises, information and assistance services covering all areas of interest to SMEs, particularly access to financing, participation in European programmes and the Community standards framework, including in the areas of the environment and employment.

Their mission is not confined to making information available, but extends to a proactive approach that can be adapted to enterprises' needs.

In that context, the EIC network has been especially active on environmental, health and safety matters. A campaign has been launched as part of the SAFE programme to promote best practice on matters of health and safety at the workplace, and considerable expertise on questions of the environment is present across the whole network.

Yet, although the diversity of the existing networks, born of different programmes and answering to specific objectives, makes it possible to respond to a wide variety of needs, it is also liable to project a heterogeneous image which can be a source of confusion to enterprises.

For that reason, and in the context of the multiannual programme for enterprises and in particular for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) (2001-2005), the Commission has undertaken to review the operation of the existing support networks with a view to improving their integration and streamlining.

That approach is consistent with the line of reducing duplication and increasing efficiency in order to guarantee better visibility and ease of access to beneficiaries by boosting the operation of these services at local and regional level.

The Commission approved a first stage in that approach on 21 February 2001.

 

Question no 86 by Pat the Cope Gallagher (H-0177/01)
 Subject: Child labour
 

The EU was closely involved in the adoption of the ILO’s new Convention on Child Labour. Will the Commission provide an up-to-date assessment of the implementation of the Convention, and is it satisfied that enough is being done to eliminate the most intolerable forms of child labour, including bonded labour? Is it satisfied that an examination of imports into the EU reflects a determination by the Member States not to trade in goods produced by child labour?

 
  
 

To date, International Labour Organisation Convention No 182 on the Worst Forms of Child Labour has been ratified by a total of 62 countries around the world, including a very large number of developing countries. The pace of ratification of the new Convention by ILO Member States overall has thus been very rapid, and according to the ILO itself has set a record compared with other ILO Conventions. The Commission is hopeful that this will contribute to the new Convention becoming increasingly recognised as a standard of universal validity.

The Commission is following with particular interest the ratification of the Convention by the Member States, notably in view of the Recommendation adopted by the Commission on 15 September 2000, which encouraged those Member States which have not yet done so to ratify the new Convention. So far, the Convention has been ratified by six Member States: Ireland, Finland, the United Kingdom, Italy, Portugal and Denmark.

While it is difficult to assess the implementation of the new Convention in practice, given that it only entered into force in November 2000, the Commission will follow closely the ILO's own assessments in this regard.

The Commission wishes to take this opportunity to stress that the elimination of child labour is a goal which it is actively pursuing in its own development policy, notably via the funding of projects specifically devoted to this issue in a number of developing countries. The Commission would further underline that the phenomenon of child labour is closely linked to poverty, and that poverty elimination is at the heart of its development policy in general. It is against this background that the Community in the trade context continues to pursue a policy of incentives to promote core labour standards, rather than sanctions, which would be counterproductive in that they risk exacerbating the social problems that they seek to redress.

 

Question no 87 by Liam Hyland (H-0179/01)
 Subject: Safeguarding the development of rural policy
 

Given the unprecedented difficult climate facing the farming community throughout the EU, will the Commission state what additional measures it considers could be introduced to protect and promote the interests of people living in the EU's rural areas?

 
  
 

The Commission fully shares the honourable Member’s concern for the difficult circumstances currently being faced by many sectors within the farming community. The Commission confirms the importance it attaches to the sustainable development of the Community’s rural areas, and to promoting thriving communities within these areas. These priorities were highlighted in the Agenda 2000 reforms, which saw rural development policy become established as the second pillar of the common agricultural policy (CAP) and ensured that Community rural development support would be extended to all rural areas within the Community.

In recent months the Commission has approved almost all of the 68 rural development programmes drawn up by Member States and regions in the context of this new, strengthened rural development policy for the period 2000-2006. These programmes contain a broad range of measures designed to improve the economic situation of both farmers and the wider community in rural areas. Given the diversity of the Community’s rural areas, much flexibility has been left to relevant Member State authorities to identify those measures best suited to meeting the specific needs of their rural communities.

The Commission will monitor closely the implementation and impact of the new generation of rural development programmes, but has no plans to introduce any specific additional instruments at the current time.

 

Question no 88 by Anna Karamanou (H-0180/01)
 Subject: Combating prostitution and drugs trafficking in the Balkans
 

The adverse economic conditions and the absence of state mechanisms in the countries of the Balkans have created a climate in which crime flourishes, particularly prostitution and drugs trafficking. In many of the new countries vast drugs trafficking networks have sprung up which work hand in hand with many national mafias. Moreover, over the last three years, the number of women falling into the hands of gangs of people traffickers has increased threefold every year: they are removed from the poor countries where they live and taken to the murky underworld of prostitution in the EU countries. What measures does the Commission intend to take to ensure that the administration of justice is better organised in the Balkan countries, that policing and cross-border controls are improved and, finally, that the countries of the EU cease to function as recipients of trafficking in human beings?

 
  
 

The fundamental objectives of the Union strategy in the Balkans are increased prosperity and a close alignment to European Standards and Laws. Establishing peace, stability and sustainable growth throughout the region is essential to achieve these objectives.

A deeper and more consistent implementation of domestic reform programs, stronger governance and institutions, better developed and integrated infrastructure are all needed to provide a solid foundation for sustainable growth and poverty reduction.

The Commission is committed to enhancing the law enforcement capacity in the Western Balkan states, both by supporting regional cooperation and by strengthening national policing and judicial bodies. The Commission is focusing on organised crime and its associated smuggling.

The objectives through the Commission's assistance program are to promote in the countries strong criminal legislation, effective investigative and border control bodies as well as powerful prosecution and judicial processes. This will be achieved by technical and legal assistance and training, by the development of modern information systems, investigative techniques and the acquisition of surveillance equipment.

In addition, other strategies and actions in the area of Justice and Home Affairs, and Employment and Social Affairs, are coordinated within the overall European approach to these problems.

This effort will help in the fight against crime, especially against the highly visible smuggling of human beings, including women and children as sexual objects, and also against drugs.

 

Question no 89 by Bart Staes (H-0182/01)
 Subject: Operating costs in the fisheries sector
 

Increased operating costs, especially fuel costs, are resulting in problems for the European fisheries sector. Accordingly, some Member States of the EU are intervening in order to reduce fuel costs for shipowners. Such divergent national and/or regional support measures result in a distortion of competition. A coordinated European approach is therefore required.

Will the Commission ensure that a coordinated European approach is adopted to the increased operating costs in the fisheries sector, given that the divergent support measures taken by national and/or regional authorities result in a distortion of competition? If not, why will the Commission not adopt a coordinated European approach?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware that the dramatic rise in fuel prices during 2000 has had a significant impact on the operating costs of fishing businesses and that a large number of those businesses, because of the rise in these operating costs, have not been able to cover all or part of their fixed costs. In view of this situation, and bearing in mind that fuel already enjoys heavy tax reductions in all Member States, several Member States have informed the Commission of their intention to compensate for the rises in the price of fuel by means of specific reductions or exemptions from certain operating costs, in particular from certain social security contributions. These Member States considered that this was an exceptional situation, which threatened the survival of fishing activity.

The Commission, while acknowledging the abrupt and sudden nature of the price rises in fuel and their harmful effect on the financial situation of fishing businesses, considers that this is not an exceptional situation. In fact, this is a fuel price level which, in real terms, has already been reached and even exceeded and which may be repeated in the future. Furthermore, the Commission believes that the impact has been all the more harshly felt since fishing is suffering from a significant excess of capacity and serious overcapitalisation. The combined effects of these factors are an increase in operating costs linked to the absence of real economies of scale, a decrease in income and, for the more modern vessels, excessive costs for finance and depreciation.

With regard to the national aid that has already been discussed and since this is operating aid, the Commission, having concluded in its preliminary enquiry that this does not seem compatible with the Treaty, has decided to open an enquiry in this respect. Furthermore, the Commission notes that this aid does not come within an overall restructuring plan aimed at re-establishing reasonable conditions for the viability of the sector in question.

It is therefore not the Commission’s intention, as the honourable Member suggests, to advocate a Community approach allowing the adoption, without any countermeasures, of specific supplementary measures to reduce fuel costs, because that would contravene the rules of the Treaty.

 

Question no 90 by Esko Olavi Seppänen (H-0185/01)
 Subject: Tackling mad cow disease
 

The Commission proposes that funds saved from the budget of the Union should be used to prevent BSE and to deal with its effects. How does the Commission justify this approach, whereby those countries where grasping farmers have not fed their cattle feed of animal origin, and which are free of BSE, would be compelled to help pay for the damage caused to the whole European meat industry by irresponsible cattle farmers and by the countries which permitted them to act as they did? Ought not each country itself to pay the costs arising from the problems it has caused?

 
  
 

It has not, as yet, been necessary to save funds from the budget in certain sectors covered by the common agricultural policy in order to free up resources to finance the measures taken to alleviate the effects of the bovine spongiform encephalopathy (BSE) crisis.

The overall budget for 2001 has, of course, been increased, and the effect of the proposals adopted by the Commission on 13 February 2001 will be to free up in the medium term funds saved from the budget in the beef and veal sector itself.

Nevertheless, in the light of market developments, and in the context of the preliminary draft budget for 2002, the Commission reserves the right to review the situation and to assess whether it would be appropriate to adopt any legislative initiatives in order to cover the potential budgetary risk incurred from the financial year 2002 onwards. Such initiatives could prove necessary in order to bring solidarity into play, something that must not always be seen as a theoretical concept.

On a more general note, the existence of a common policy and a single market requires solidarity between the Member States as well as between the different market sectors. When a market is affected by specific conditions, even within a single Member State, all the others are affected and the financial implications are suffered by everyone.

 

Question no 91 by Cristina García-Orcoyen Tormo (H-0186/01)
 Subject: National Hydrological Plan
 

Over the last few days the Spanish media have reported on the visit made by the leader of the Aragon Regional Government to various EU Commissioners in order to express his opposition to Spain's National Hydrological Plan.

The Commissioner for the Environment, Mrs Wallström, has apparently stated that the NHP is not in line with the framework directive on water.

It is also reported that the Directorate-General for the Environment has begun to investigate the environmental issues raised by the NHP and that Mrs Wallström is to take some major decisions concerning the NHP.

The author of this question has no doubts regarding the high degree of professionalism and diligence displayed by the Directorate-General for the Environment or regarding its absolute independence and its strictly technical and legal approach to matters.

This fact should be made quite clear to the people of Spain since it relates to a matter of the utmost significance and importance, in respect of which the general public deserves to be accurately informed.

Has the Commission expressed any view to the effect that Spain's National Hydrological Plan contravenes the framework directive on water and what action (if any) does the Commission intend to take with regard to this Plan?

 
  
 

First of all, I would like to make it quite clear that the Commission has not issued any opinion to the effect that Spain's National Hydrological Plan contravenes the framework directive on water.

I refer the honourable Member to the Commission's answer to Written Question E-0003/01, which deals with the same subject (the National Hydrological Plan), and to European Parliament and Council Directive 2000/60/E(1)C1 of 23 October 2000 establishing a Framework for Community Action in the field of Water Policy.

The honourable Member is also referred to the answers given by the Commission to Oral Questions H-0843/00 and H-0904/00 during the respective Question Times of the Parliamentary November and December 2000 part-sessions, which also deal with the subject of the National Hydrological Plan.

This Plan has already been the subject of a number of exchanges of information between the Commission and the Spanish authorities. The Spanish authorities have reiterated their undertaking to respect the applicable Community law during all stages of the Plan's implementation.

The Commission will have to ensure that the Plan does not contravene any Community legislation in any of the areas concerned.

 
 

(1)1 O.J. L 327 of 22.12.2000, p. 1-73.

 

Question no 92 by Alejandro Agag Longo (H-0187/01)
 Subject: Compatibility of functions
 

It was recently reported in the Spanish press (‘El Pais’ of 6 February 2001) that the federal executive committee of the Spanish Socialist Workers’ Party had privately requested Mr Domingo Jiménez Beltrán, director of the European Environment Agency, to draw up an alternative project to the national hydrological plan devised by the Spanish Government.

Is the Commission aware of this fact? Has Mr Jiménez Beltrán submitted the relevant request, pursuant to Article 12 of the Staff Regulations governing officials of the European Communities?

If so, could the Commission say when the request was lodged and what form it took? If no request has been submitted, will the Commission consider bringing proceedings against Mr Jiménez Beltrán for this irregularity?

Leaving aside legal considerations, does the Commission think that this request is compatible with the tasks and responsibilities assigned to the director of the European Environment Agency?

 
  
 

The Commission has no knowledge of any request to the Director of the European Environment Agency, Mr Jiménez Beltrán, to conduct an alternative project to the National Hydrological Plan devised by the Spanish Government. On the contrary, in his open letter to the Spanish Minister for the Environment, Mr Jaume Matas, of 12 February 2001 (Annex), Mr Jiménez Beltrán states clearly that he has never received such a request. Consequently, the Commission has not received and did not need to receive any request by Mr Jiménez Beltrán pursuant to Article 12 of the Staff Regulations.

The Director of the EEA is accountable for his activities to the Management Board of the EEA.

In his above mentioned letter of 12 February 2001, Mr Jiménez Beltrán states that he should not and could not, in his position, have accepted and implemented such request if ever received. The Commission is of the opinion that this attitude is consistent with Mr Jiménez Beltrán’s duties and tasks.

 

Question no 93 by Ioannis Souladakis (H-0194/01)
 Subject: United States missile defence
 

In a recent interview published on 6 February 2001 in the International Herald Tribune, George Robertson, NATO Secretary-General, said that NATO was the most suitable institution to discuss the development of the missile defence system which George Bush, the newly-elected President of the USA, has brought back into the news with plans for its development.

In view of both the extreme importance of this issue for European countries and the creation of the CFSP within the European Union framework:

Does the Commission consider that limiting discussions on this subject exclusively to the NATO setting will produce conclusions without the Union's participation at a time when the EU is establishing the CFSP?

Is the Commission examining the possibility of bringing the subject up for discussion with the European Union to determine the Member States' common positions on this issue?

What is the Commission's position on the topic, in view of the objections to the US leadership's intentions from a number of EU Member States, NATO and other countries?

 
  
 

National Missile Defence is about territorial defence, and the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation (NATO) is in the business of territorial defence, so of course that is the appropriate forum for a broader debate between allies. And no, the Commission is not worried that the European point of view would be lost in such a debate. After all, all but two of NATO’s members are European, and more than half of them are EU Member States.

On the substance, the Commissioner responsible for External Relations has already some time ago stated: "The US, because of its boundless confidence in technology, its pre-eminence as a world power and its geographical position, can contemplate technical solutions - such as National Missile Defence - to the threats that it faces. Whatever scepticism or enthusiasm one may have about this approach - and for what it’s worth I remain to be wholly convinced - it is symptomatic of a belief that the world can be kept at bay. Interestingly, this belief has increased US reliance on tools (military threats and action) which, in Europe, are a Member State responsibility. In Europe, by contrast, our geography rules out such an approach, even if we were to believe in it. We can only achieve security by engaging constructively with our nearest neighbours."

 

Question no 94 by María Antonia Avilés Perea (H-0195/01)
 Subject: Opinions expressed on the web page of the European Environment Agency
 

Via the web page of the European Environment Agency it is very easy to gain access to the opinion which, on a personal basis (the author states on several occasions that his opinion 'does not constitute the formal opinion of the Agency, the Commission or other Community institutions'), has been expressed by the Director of the Agency, Domingo Jiménez Beltrán, concerning the National Hydrological Plan drawn up by the Spanish Government.

Does the Commission not consider that, in view of the political significance of the issue and the way in which such views may skew the debate, it would be better if Mr Jiménez Beltrán's opinion were to be taken off the EEA's web page, especially since it does not reflect the Agency's official views?

Does this state of affairs not contravene Articles 11 and 12 of the Staff Regulations applicable to EU officials, particularly in view of the fact that the web page in question contains a section reserved for the Director?

 
  
 

The opinion of Mr Jiménez Beltrán on the Spanish National Hydrological Plan has been taken, in the normal course of its monthly publication, from the EEA’s web page already in January 2001.

The Commission understands that it is usual practice to put for a limited period, on the EEA web page conferences, presentations and articles that Mr Jiménez Beltrán does in the areas of environment and sustainable development. These publications on the web page are meant to provide additional information for the public on subjects that fall into the range of activities of the agency.

The Director of the EEA may publish his opinions while respecting his obligation of loyalty under the Staff Regulations.

The agency has legal autonomy and under the EEA-regulation the Director is accountable for his activities to the EEA-Management Board. Therefore it is not for the Commission to make any judgement of whether Mr Jiménez Beltrán has or has not overstepped his obligations under Article 12 of the Staff Regulations with regard to the agency.

The Commission will – through its member of the Management Board – suggest that the Board should establish clear guidance about the information to be put on this web site in future.

 

Question no 95 by Carmen Cerdeira Morterero (H-0199/01)
 Subject: El Ejido
 

In its resolution of 17 February 2000 concerning the incidents which occurred at El Ejido on 5 to 7 February 2000, the European Parliament welcomed the agreement reached by all the parties concerned (Spanish Government, Andalusia regional government, El Ejido local authorities, businessmen, trade unions, immigrants' associations, etc.) concerning a series of measures to resolve the problem of providing assistance for health, education and housing.

Will the Commission examine the causes and practical effects of these incidents and the measures adopted, to ensure that events of this kind, which run counter to the values represented by the European Union, do not recur?

 
 

Question no 96 by Glyn Ford (H-0203/01)
 Subject: Immigration policy - El Ejido
 

The racism that flared up in El Ejido between 5 and 7 February 2000 illustrated the difficulties facing the European Union: large­scale immigration of workers, economic development and a lack of dialogue and understanding between communities. The problems have not gone away, and could trigger further crises, both in Andalusia and in other regions of Europe. Does the Commission not believe that the time has now come to hold a far­reaching debate on immigration policy, addressing all aspects of the question, rather than adopting a piecemeal and frequently restrictive approach towards the issues involved?

 
 

Question no 97 by Thierry Cornillet (H-0214/01)
 Subject: Follow-up to the events in El Ejido
 

What does the Commission intend to do to ensure that the housing conditions of a large number of horticultural workers in El Ejido in Andalusia respect the provisions of the Charter of Fundamental Rights and do not constitute an affront to human dignity?

 
 

Question no 98 by Pedro Marset Campos (H-0216/01)
 Subject: Immigrant workers in El Ejido
 

What steps does the Commission intend to take to ensure that immigration is handled more humanely, particularly as regards sectors employing large number of immigrant workers, such as the horticultural industry in El Ejido in Andalusia, so as to facilitate the integration of these communities?

 
 

Question no 99 by Fodé Sylla (H-0218/01)
 Subject: El Ejido: why have there been no prosecutions?
 

On 5-7 February 2000, extremely serious incidents occurred in El Ejido in Andalusia. Both large numbers of eye witnesses and the media stated that the riots were racist and xenophobic in nature, and the European Parliament adopted a resolution severely censuring what had taken place. What does the Commission intend to do in view of the fact that there have been no prosecutions or sentences in connection with the events at El Ejido a year ago, although almost 700 complaints have been lodged?

Joint Answer

1. The Commission shares the honourable Members' concerns about the serious incidents that occurred in El Ejido last year, and firmly condemns any form of expression of racist or xenophobic views which may constitute a violation of the human rights and fundamental freedoms which are common to all the Member States.

2. The Commission welcomes the agreement reached in El Ejido by the Spanish authorities, the employers, unions and immigrant associations concerning the improvements of the living and working conditions of immigrants in the area.

3. This serious incident illustrates very well the problems which arise from uncontrolled migration into the Community. This is one of the reasons why the Commission adopted, on 22 November 2000, a Communication on a Community Immigration Policy(1) which is designed to stimulate a wide-ranging debate in the Member States on a new approach to immigration, This Communication was presented at the same time as a separate Communication setting out the different policy options for the establishment of a common asylum policy(2).

4. The Communication on immigration sets out progress already made with the implementation of the different elements of an asylum and immigration policy for the Union already agreed at Tampere and puts forward a new approach to labour migration, taking into account the changed demographic and economic context of the Union and of the countries of origin. It proposes that the current 'zero immigration' policies of the last 20-30 years be abandoned and that a controlled migration policy, which both reflects labour market needs and new patterns and pressures of immigration flows on the Union, should be developed.

5. The Communication emphasises that such a policy must include equal treatment of migrants admitted, especially with respect to living and working conditions, and consequently also further efforts to combat illegal work.

6. As regards the integration of immigrant communities, the Council has adopted, on a Commission proposal, a directive(3) aimed at prohibiting, in all Member States, discrimination on the basis of race or ethnic origin in a number of areas such as employment, education, social security, health care, and access to goods and services, including accommodation. Member States have until 19 July 2003 to adopt the national measures necessary to bring themselves into line with that directive.

7. As regards criminal legislation in this area, the Commission intends to refer to the Council, in the course of the year 2001, a legislative proposal on common definitions, charges and penalties for offences related to racism and xenophobia. Once that proposal has been adopted by the Council, it will be the responsibility of the Member States to adopt the necessary measures to bring themselves into line with the provisions of that instrument. In this connection, the Commission is concerned to point out that it has no authority over the administration of justice in the Member States and that any decision to bring criminal proceedings is a matter for the judicial authorities in those states.

8. The Commission wishes to reiterate its commitment to combating racist and xenophobic phenomena by all the means laid down in the Treaties. These activities were reinforced by the activities of the European Monitoring Centre on Racism and Xenophobia, set up in June 1997 in Vienna. The Observatory is an independent body that can draw up conclusions and opinions for forwarding to the Community and its Member States, and publishes an annual report on the situation of racism and xenophobia in the 15 Member States. Implementation of those actions can be expected to help to secure respect for and protection of human dignity, which is, of course, one of the basic principles of the European Union's Charter of Fundamental Rights(4), to which one of the honourable Members refers.

 
 
 

(1) COM (2000) 0757
(2) COM (2000) 0755
(3) Council Directive 2000/43/EC implementing the principle of equal treatment between persons irrespective of racial or ethnic origin
- OJ L 180, 19 July 2000, p. 22
(4) OJ C 364, 18 December 2000

 

Question no 100 by Pernille Frahm (H-0200/01)
 Subject: Motorway through EU Habitat no. 45
 

Does the Commission consider it responsible to plan a motorway from Århus to Ringkøbing through EU Habitat no. 45 covering the Gudenå Valley and the Gjern Bakker, even though there is quite clearly an alternative route for the motorway around the outside of the area; or will it recommend that Denmark should not waste many resources on planning and on studying the possibilities for this road through a protected nature reserve; and will it speedily answer the question from Stefan Rossel of 3 October 2000 to that effect, with a copy to the Danish government?

 
  
 

The proposed Natura 2000 site no 45 by Denmark covers a part of the Gudenå valley and Gjern Bakker hills. According to the Commission database, Community interest is linked to two habitat types and three species. These habitat types are European Dry Heaths (code n° 4030) and Old Acidophilus Oak Woods (code n° 9190), and the species concerned are the otter (Lutra lutra), a bat Myotis dasycneme and a salamander Triuturus cristatus.

According to Article 6 of the Habitats Directive (43/92) any plan or project likely to have a significant effect on a Natura 2000 site shall be assessed in view of the site's conservation objectives. The competent authorities shall agree to the plan or project only after having ascertained that it will not adversely affect the integrity of the site concerned.

If this assessment is negative and alternative solutions do not exist, a plan or project may nevertheless be carried out only for imperative reasons of overriding public interest. In this case, Member States shall take compensatory measures to ensure that the overall coherence of Natura 2000 is protected.

Only under these conditions it is possible to implement a plan or project affecting a Nature 2000 site.

The Commission will respond to Mr Rossel´s letter in the near future.

 

Question no 101 by Jens-Peter Bonde (H-0201/01)
 Subject: The subsidiarity principle and local bus services in Århus
 

Does the Commission consider it compatible with the principle of democracy that EU rules should be able to prevent the Århus Town Council from organising local bus services in the way it has decided in accordance with a democratic mandate from the voters?

Does the Commission consider it compatible with the subsidiarity principle that EU rules can be used to force a local authority to put its local bus services out to tender when the bus company does not have any effect on price trends in other EU countries?

 
  
 

In line with the principle of subsidiarity, it is for local, regional and national authorities to decide on the pattern of public transport services they believe their area needs; to decide in what ways these services need to be integrated with each other; to decide on the division of roles and responsibilities between authorities and public transport operators; and to select the operator or operators that will fulfil their public service requirements.

The Community has neither the capacity nor the competence to take over these tasks.

However these tasks, like other actions in the field of services of general interest, need to be carried out in a way that takes into account the requirements of the rules of the Treaty, notably those relating to state aids and to the award of exclusive rights.

In general, these rules come into force only where a particular measure has a potential impact on trade between Member States.

In the past, public transport in the different Member States was organised on purely national or even local lines. Operators were active only within a single Member State; there were no international operators of local and regional public transport.

During the last decade, this situation has completely changed. The majority of Member States, including Denmark, have chosen to open at least part of their public transport sector to competition. International as well as national operators have taken advantage of this opportunity to enter new markets – again, Denmark is an example.

As a result, the organisation of public transport is no longer a matter that has purely local effects. In this emerging single market, abuses of Treaty rules on state aids or the award of exclusive rights can now have a direct effect on trade between Member States.

This is already the case as Community law stands today. However, the full implications of these Treaty rules in the public transport sector are not clear, and authorities and operators are experiencing legal uncertainty. The Commission has therefore made a proposal for a regulation on public service requirements in public transport. The aim of this proposal is to remove this legal uncertainty. Moreover, the proposal aims to ensure, through a system of controlled competition, that public authorities in the Member States have explicit powers to deliver quality and integration in public transport and to protect existing employees in the sector.

The evidence shows that competition, properly regulated, is an effective way to promote these objectives. It is certainly true that public companies often offer services of a high quality, and the Commission’s objective is not to promote privatisation. However it is appropriate for these companies, like any others, to show from time to time that they are still the best for the job of delivering services in their area. Such a demonstration is in the interest of passengers and of the Community at large.

 

Question no 102 by Marit Paulsen (H-0204/01)
 Subject: Applicant country involvement in shaping EU food legislation
 

In its White Paper on food safety (COM(1999)0719/final), the Commission describes food safety in the European Union as a key policy priority. In addition to establishing a European Food Authority, it proposes over 80 different radical measures to be adopted over the next five years with the aim of improving legislation in this field and making it more coherent.

According to the Commission, the best way to restore consumer confidence in food in the European Union is to increase transparency and public participation in the decision-making process. Negotiations with the applicant countries are progressing at the same time as rapid changes are taking place in EU food legislation. How has the Commission involved the applicant countries in the shaping of the new food legislation, which is, after all, expected to be in place when the first new Member States join the Union?

 
  
 

Food safety plays an important role in the preparation for enlargement. Emphasis is put on the existing acquis and on early implementation. It is essential that the candidate countries adapt their legislation as early as possible to Community rules in order to ensure the highest possible consumer protection within an enlarged future Community. Consequently implementation of food safety legislation is a key pre-requisite for a successful completion of the enlargement negotiations.

The Commission has continuously stressed that administrative structures as well as the operators should implement Community rules as early as possible and take advantage of the technical aid offered during the pre-accession period. To this end all candidate countries receive yearly, a summary of all new legislation added to the existing acquis. Furthermore the Commission organises regular screening exercises to highlight or explain further the new rules in detail.

The proposals of the White Paper have been presented on various occasions. However, it would be premature for the candidate countries to take full account of such proposals, prior to their adoption. As and when, the measures set out in the White Paper become law, they will be presented in the screening process mentioned above. However and in addition to the early implementation it is crucial that the candidate countries adopt already today to the “farm to table approach”, which is the central theme of the White Paper on Food Safety. This point is particularly important as the relevant food safety legislation falls under two very different chapters for which the negotiations are conducted separately: chapter 1 (free movement of goods) which covers the general food safety rules and chapter 7 (agriculture) which covers more particularly the rules on food of animal origin.

The Commission has invited all candidate countries to present a "food safety strategy " setting out their existing or future measures intended to enable them to adapt to the Community food safety system. It has emphasised that it is imperative for the candidate countries’ to provide a coherent timetable for transposition, implementation and enforcement of the acquis communautaire, and that it is essential to demonstrate how they intend to ensure a coherent approach throughout the food chain, whatever chapter is concerned.

Of course the “food safety strategy”, which they should transmit in April, is aiming at a “moving target” as the Community acquis is under constant development. The discussions that will follow the transmission of their “food safety strategy” will address new elements as the Community acquis is adapted over time.

 

Question no 103 by Ioannis Patakis (H-0206/01)
 Subject: Peach producers
 

In admirable cooperation with commercial manufacturers and processors and certain self-appointed representatives of peach producers, the Greek Government is violating the regulation on clingstone peaches and not paying the prescribed minimum price on all quantities of clingstone peaches sold by peach producers to processing manufacturers.

What steps does the Commission intend to take to ensure that the regulation is properly applied and that the minimum price is paid immediately to peach producers on all quantities of clingstone peaches which they have sold to processors?

 
  
 

Within the framework of the system of production aid for peach processors established until the peach-growing year 2000/2001 by Regulation (EC) No 2201/96, Community aid is only provided on the condition that the processor has paid the producer, or, in the majority of cases, the producer organisations which have in turn to pay the member producers, the whole of the minimum price fixed by the Community.

If the contracts between producers or producer organisations and peach processors do not stipulate at least this minimum Community price, or if this price is not effectively paid to the producers for the quantity in question, then the aid is not due.

As of the peach-growing year 2001/2002, the aid system has been altered by Regulation No 2699/2000, to the effect that Community aid is to be paid directly to the producer organisations or, where appropriate, to the individual producers. The minimum price has therefore been removed.

 

Question no 104 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0207/01)
 Subject: Olive oil
 

Two and a half years since the implementation of Regulation (EC) 1638/98(1) (the new regulation on oil), one of whose actions was to abolish the intervention arrangements, the olive oil market has collapsed, with nominal producers’ prices for the 2000 crop year 40% lower than prices for the three-year period from 1995 to 1997, before the new regulation was implemented. In the recent new hefty proposal for amendments to the regulation submitted by the Commission, there are no signs of concern or of any measures to restrain the predatory exploitation of olive producers by the multinationals, or to protect their incomes.

Does the Commission intend to examine this undesirable state of affairs, where the few remaining farmers in poor and barren island regions, who produce an extremely wholesome dietary product, are threatened with wholesale, violent extermination? Does it intend to take steps to protect the price of olive oil and the incomes of olive producers?

 
  
 

For some months, the market prices for olive oil have been falling quite sharply. This drop is caused by two factors: a cyclical production surplus due to a substantial harvest, and the arrival on the market, in addition to the good harvest, of oil from recently established olive groves which are gradually reaching maturity.

This production surplus, which is likely almost to become structural, cannot be resolved through simple storage measures. Sooner or later, the stock would have to be placed on the market, and this would exacerbate the problem.

Nevertheless, in order to provide temporary relief for the market, the Commission has authorised producers in the regions worst affected by falling prices to make use of private storage, with the result that 100 000 tonnes can currently be stored privately in Spain and Greece.

The Commission does not feel that the right solution to the difficulties lies in supporting supply but in encouraging demand, by means, in particular, of a quality strategy. This is what it outlined in its December 2000 report on the matter.

As far as the growers of the island regions of Greece are concerned, the Commission points out that they benefit from special measures and aid.

 
 

(1) OJ L 210, 28.7.1998, p. 32.

 

Question no 105 by Johannes (Hans) Blokland (H-0208/01)
 Subject: Reproductive health definition
 

According to the World Health Organisation(1), the term Reproductive Health includes the term ‘Fertility Regulation’ which includes ‘interrupting unwanted pregnancies’.

With regard to the reference to reproductive health in various items in the budget for 2001, how does the Commission define the term Reproductive Health? Does it include abortion?

 
  
 

Various Community actions within the public health framework (budget line B3-4308) support work directly or indirectly related to reproductive health. These include:

1) Surveillance, prevention and control of infectious diseases impacting on reproductive health, in particular of Sexually Transmitted Diseases (STD);

2) Actions aimed at addressing health determinants impacting on reproductive health, such as alcohol and tobacco;

3) Surveillance of congenital anomalies in the framework of the rare diseases programme.

None of the currently running eight public health programmes nor the proposed new public health programme include actions on abortion.

 
 

(1) World Health Organisation, Definition and Indicators in Family Planning, Maternal and Child Health and Reproductive Health, used in the WHO Regional Office for Europe, revised March 1999.

 

Question no 106 by Yasmine Boudjenah (H-0210/01)
 Subject: Community assistance to refugees
 

In 1995 the Community contributed 21% of the budget of the United Nations High Commission for Refugees. In 2000 the figure was just 5%. The contributions of the Member States do not come close to compensating for this reduction.

At a time when there are growing numbers of refugees in the world, does the Commission intend to propose a substantial increase in the Community's contribution?

 
  
 

The Commission provided the third largest contribution to UNHCR in 2000. It is true that there has been a decrease in Community funding to the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) in recent years from the peaks of mid-1990s., This has not been the result of any policy decision by the Commission.

It rather corresponds to a gradual decrease in UNHCR’s caseload, the changing nature of crises in the late 1990s (generating more internationally displaced persons (IDPs) and less refugees), as well as the number and quality of projects submitted for Community funding.

The overall contribution of the Community to UNHCR has remained around 40% of major donors throughout the 1990s. In 2000, the Community contribution to UNHCR was EUR 66 million. It ended up ranking third highest after the United States and Japan. At any rate, the Commission acknowledges the inadequacy of the current, financially ‘unpredictable’ working relationship with UNHCR and is actively addressing that unsatisfactory situation, aiming at providing appropriate support through a better-structured dialogue in exchange for up-to-standard performance. A change of the Financial Regulation – as proposed by the Commission – that will allow a programme based funding will contribute to this.

However, although Community funding will remain subject to the submission of quality proposals and the rendering of quality services by UNHCR, the Commission is foreseeing an increase in the contribution to UNHCR.

 

Question no 107 by Nelly Maes (H-0223/01)
 Subject: Government pressure on the press in Austria
 

The international press rights association, 'Reporters Without Frontiers', has published a report saying that press freedom is under substantial pressure in Austria. Is the Commission aware of this lack of press freedom? If not, will it look into this lack of press freedom? If the Commission is aware of the problem, is it prepared to take action to help guarantee freedom of the press in Austria?

Nevertheless, measures such as those called for in the question from the honourable Member are the responsibility of the Austrian authorities. In the present state of Community law, such measures do not come within the Commission's responsibility. Moreover, the conditions for the application of the Union Treaty provisions concerning the violation of fundamental rights have not been met in this case

 

Question no 108 by Karin Riis-Jørgensen (H-0227/01)
 Subject: Export aid application fees in Denmark
 

The Danish government has issued a decree (No 1344 of 18 December 2000) concerning fees for applications for aid in respect of exports of foodstuffs, which took effect on 1 January 2001.

These new Danish rules mean that a Danish firm must pay a DKK 200 fee each time it is entitled to receive export aid, irrespective of the amount of such aid.

Does the Commission consider the imposition by a Member State of such competition-distorting fees on its firms to be compatible with the rules governing the internal market?

Will it also state whether the Danish government has notified these new rules in the form outlined above, as they differ from the ones originally communicated to it, and will it lastly say whether the requirement for Danish firms to pay such fees can be regarded as anything other than a tax on exports?

 
  
 

The question of the honourable Member concerns whether the Commission considers the administrative fees introduced in Denmark on applications for export refunds as compatible with Community law, in particular the rules of the internal market.

At the time when the fee scheme was planned, the Danish authorities informed the Commission although there was no obligation to notify the national rules to the Commission. The now adopted legislation is not on any major points different from the project presented to the Commission. The relevant Community legislation, considered in the light of the jurisprudence of the Court of Justice, gives the Member States a degree of latitude for the introduction of such fees. The fee scheme does not appear to have an impact on the internal market. Nor do the fees seem to be equivalent to a tax on exports, since they are intended to cover the administrative costs related to the payment of a Community aid.

The concerned fee scheme is at present subject to a complaint investigated by the Commission. The Commission will inform the honourable Member of the outcome of this investigation.

 

Question no 109 by John Joseph McCartin (H-0230/01)
 Subject: Differentials in pre-tax car prices
 

Is the Commission aware of the vast difference in the price of pre-tax cars of the same model in the various Member States of the EU? Is this differential a violation of European consumer rights? Has the Commission any plans to resolve this problem?

 
  
 

The Commission has, since 1993, monitored the evolution of car price differentials in the Community. In its recent evaluation report on the application of this Regulation, the Commission dedicates a section to this issue. A study by independent experts for the purpose of this report analyses certain factors influencing car price differentials in more detail. It comes to the conclusion that a part of such differentials is due to fiscal measures and exchange rate fluctuations, while the remaining portion cannot be explained. The question of car price differentials will be an element to be taken into account in the Commission's considerations on the future regime for motor vehicle distribution. Price differentials as such may not constitute a violation of competition rules and the Commission has no power to oblige car manufacturers to apply certain prices. It can, however, secure that consumers' right to purchase a new car in the Member State of their choice, as provided in the above Regulation is not hindered, and will prosecute any violation of this right.

 

Question no 110 by Antonios Trakatellis (H-0236/01)

Question no 111 by Konstantinos Alyssandrakis (H-0242/01)
 Subject: Resumption of operations at a quarry of building materials in the commune of Marmara, Ioannina
 

A quarry for building materials situated at the edge of the urban area (at a distance of 300 metres) of one of the largest and most historic municipalities in Greece, that of Ioannina, has recently resumed operations. Apart from the fact that the quarry has turned the commune of Marmara (one of the oldest in the region which is mentioned in the Golden Bull of the Byzantine Emperor Andronikos II Palaeologos of 1318) into a wasteland, it is also destroying and levelling the hill on which the monastery of Aghia Paraskevi, dating from 1778, is situated. What measures does the Commission intend to take to preserve the monuments of the historical and cultural heritage of the region and protect the life and health of inhabitants and the environment in general?

In order to identify the Community legislation applicable to the subject and to be able to conduct an investigation, a description of the type of quarry is necessary, together with the date it first went into operation and the text on the basis of which it has been authorised to resume operations.

 

Question no 112 by Miet Smet (H-0243/01)
 Subject: Aid for training measures
 

In Belgium, the social partners in certain sectors have agreed to pay a proportion of the total payroll into funds which finance training measures. The resources involved are not State funds, but the State has declared them compulsory by Royal Decree. The resources are therefore used to cofinance measures which also receive support from the ESF. Does Recital 6 of Commission Regulation (EC) 68/2001(1) mean that, henceforth, the Commission will regard such contributions as State aid? If that were to be the case, does the Commission realise that the sectoral social partners involved will be inclined to use such resources solely for their own company training schemes or for purposes other than training and not for training schemes which involve more than one company or are not directly applicable?

 
  
 

As sectoral training funds in Belgium (as well as in some other Member States) are made compulsory by way of national legislation they must legally be regarded as State resources within the meaning of Article 87 (1) EC Treaty and constitute State aid if the other conditions of this Article are met. As a consequence they are in principle notifiable to the Commission for advance approval pursuant to Article 88 (3) EC Treaty.

In respect to training aid, two important exceptions to the notification obligation should be pointed out:

Aid which complies with the 'de minimis' rule (when the amount of aid involved does not exceed the ceiling of EUR 100,000 over a three-year period) is not notifiable.

Aid which falls within the applicable levels specified in the Exemption Regulation on Training Aid (Regulation 68/2001) is no longer notifiable provided all relevant conditions are complied with. This should significantly reduce the incidence of notifications required relating to Belgian sectoral training funds: only all individual projects which involve expenditure in excess of EUR 1 million has to be notified to the Commission.

In the Commission’s view, training usually has positive external effects for society as a whole since it increases the pool of skilled workers from which other firms may draw, improves the competitiveness of Community industry and plays an important role in the employment strategy. In view of the fact that enterprises in the Community generally under-invest in the training of their workers, State aid might help to correct this market imperfection and therefore can be considered (under certain conditions) to be compatible with the common market and therefore exempted from prior notification.

The Commission is therefore of the opinion that Regulation 68/2001 is an important step forward since it considerably simplifies the procedural obligations of Member States who want to grant training aid. Regarding the use of sectoral training funds, the Regulation has clarified the existing situation and thereby provided a transparent framework for sectoral training funds.

In recently approved Objective 3 Single Programming Documents in Belgium, it is clearly stated that sectoral training funds (which fall within the applicable levels specified in the Exemption Regulation on Training Aid - Regulation 68/2001) continue to be used as cofinancing for ESF projects.

 
 

(1) OJ L 10, 13.1.2001, p. 20.

 

Question no 113 by Bárbara Dührkop Dührkop (H-0247/01)
 Subject: Communication of information on air accidents and their causes in Spain
 

Under Article 9 of Directive 94/56/EC(1), Member States must forward to the Commission reports of air accidents occurring on their territory and recommendations resulting from investigations into them.

How many such reports and recommendations has the Spanish Government submitted to the Commission since March 1998? Will the Commission give details thereof?

 
  
 

To date, the Commission has not received any inquiry reports from the Spanish government on air accidents occurring on its territory.

The Commission has officially requested the Spanish authorities to provide it with a list of accidents and incidents that should be the subject of an inquiry under Directive 94/56/EC, which have occurred since the entry into force of Royal Decree 389/1998 of 13 March 1998 transposing Directive 94/56/EC into Spanish law.

The Commission has also requested that it be sent copies of the inquiry reports and recommendations which have been published since that date.

 
 

(1) OJ L 319, 12.12.1994, p.14.
